\subsection{Closure Properties}

\subsubsection{Union}

Formally, 

\[L(M_1) \cup L(M_2) = \{x \in \Sigma^* \mid x \in L(M_1) \lor x \in L(M_2)\}\]

Given $M_1 = (W_1, \Sigma, V_1, S_1, F_1)$ and $M_2 = (W_2, \Sigma, V_2, S_2, F_2)$, the union is constructed as:

\[M = (W_1 \times W_2, \Sigma, V, (S_1, S_2), F)\]

where

\[F = \{(w_1, w_2) \mid w_1 \in F_1 \lor w_2 \in F_2\}\]

and

\[V((w_1, w_2), x) = (V_1(w_1, x), V_2(w_2, x))\]

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ containing at least one $1$,  
and let $L(M_2)$ be the set of strings containing at least one $0$.

The union language is:
\[
L(M_1) \cup L(M_2) = \{ x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains at least one } 1 \text{ or one } 0 \}.
\]

\textbf{DFA} $M_1$: Accepts strings with at least one $1$.

$M_1 = (W_1,\Sigma,V_1,S_1,F_1)$ where

\[
W_1 = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_1 = w_0, \quad
F_1 = \{w_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};

    \path[->]
        (w0) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1)
        (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{DFA} $M_2$: Accepts strings with at least one $0$.

$M_2 = (W_2,\Sigma,V_2,S_2,F_2)$ where

\[
W_2 = \{v_0,v_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_2 = v_0, \quad
F_2 = \{v_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (v0) {$v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (v1) [right=of v0] {$v_1$};

    \path[->]
        (v0) edge[bend right] node {0} (v1)
        (v0) edge[loop above] node {1} ()
        (v1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Union Construction:}

\[
M = M_1 \cup M_2 = (W_1 \times W_2,\ \Sigma,\ V,\ (w_0,v_0),\ F)
\]

where

\[
W_1 \times W_2 = \{(w_0,v_0), (w_0,v_1), (w_1,v_0), (w_1,v_1)\}
\]

and

\[
F = \{(x,y) \mid x \in F_1 \lor y \in F_2\} = \{(w_0,v_1), (w_1,v_0), (w_1,v_1)\}.
\]

The transition function $V$ is defined by:

\[
V((w_i,v_j), a) = (V_1(w_i, a), V_2(v_j, a))
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.8cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (00) {$w_0,v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (10) [right=of 00] {$w_1,v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (01) [below=of 00] {$w_0,v_1$};
    \node[state, accepting] (11) [right=of 01] {$w_1,v_1$};

    \path[->]
        (00) edge node[above] {1} (10)
        (00) edge node[left] {0} (01)

        (10) edge node[right] {0} (11)
        (10) edge[loop right] node {1} ()
        
        (01) edge node[below] {1} (11)
        (01) edge[loop left] node {0} ()
        
        (11) edge[loop right] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

The product state $(w_i, v_j)$ represents ``$M_1$ in state $w_i$ and $M_2$ in state $v_j$''. A state is accepting if \emph{either} component is in an accepting state (union semantics).

Thus,

\[
L(M_1) \cup L(M_2) = L(M) = \{x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \neq \varepsilon\}.
\]

\textbf{Verification:}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\varepsilon$: Neither $M_1$ nor $M_2$ accepts $\to$ rejected 
    \item $1$: $M_1$ accepts $\to$ accepted 
    \item $0$: $M_2$ accepts $\to$ accepted 
    \item $10$: Both accept $\to$ accepted 
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{Intersection}

Formally,

\[L(M_1) \cap L(M_2) = \{x \in \Sigma^* \mid x \in L(M_1) \land x \in L(M_2)\}\]

Given $M_1 = (W_1, \Sigma, V_1, S_1, F_1)$ and $M_2 = (W_2, \Sigma, V_2, S_2, F_2)$, the intersection is constructed as:

\[M = (W_1 \times W_2, \Sigma, V, (S_1, S_2), F)\]

where

\[F = \{(w_1, w_2) \mid w_1 \in F_1 \land w_2 \in F_2\}\]

and

\[V((w_1, w_2), x) = (V_1(w_1, x), V_2(w_2, x))\]

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ containing at least one $1$,  
and let $L(M_2)$ be the set of strings containing at least one $0$.

The intersection language is:
\[
L(M_1) \cap L(M_2) = \{ x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains at least one } 1 \text{ and at least one } 0 \}.
\]

\textbf{DFA} $M_1$: Accepts strings with at least one $1$.

$M_1 = (W_1,\Sigma,V_1,S_1,F_1)$ where

\[
W_1 = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_1 = w_0, \quad
F_1 = \{w_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};

    \path[->]
        (w0) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1)
        (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{DFA} $M_2$: Accepts strings with at least one $0$.

$M_2 = (W_2,\Sigma,V_2,S_2,F_2)$ where

\[
W_2 = \{v_0,v_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_2 = v_0, \quad
F_2 = \{v_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (v0) {$v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (v1) [right=of v0] {$v_1$};

    \path[->]
        (v0) edge[bend right] node {0} (v1)
        (v0) edge[loop above] node {1} ()
        (v1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Intersection Construction:}

\[
M = M_1 \cap M_2 = (W_1 \times W_2,\ \Sigma,\ V,\ (w_0,v_0),\ F)
\]

where

\[
W_1 \times W_2 = \{(w_0,v_0), (w_0,v_1), (w_1,v_0), (w_1,v_1)\}
\]

and

\[
F = \{(x,y) \mid x \in F_1 \land y \in F_2\} = \{(w_1,v_1)\}.
\]

The transition function $V$ is defined by:

\[
V((w_i,v_j), a) = (V_1(w_i, a), V_2(v_j, a))
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.8cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (00) {$w_0,v_0$};
    \node[state] (10) [right=of 00] {$w_1,v_0$};
    \node[state] (01) [below=of 00] {$w_0,v_1$};
    \node[state, accepting] (11) [right=of 01] {$w_1,v_1$};

    \path[->]
        (00) edge node[above] {1} (10)
        (00) edge node[left] {0} (01)

        (10) edge node[right] {0} (11)
        (10) edge[loop right] node {1} ()

        (01) edge node[below] {1} (11)
        (01) edge[loop left] node {0} ()

        (11) edge[loop right] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

The product state $(w_i, v_j)$ represents ``$M_1$ in state $w_i$ and $M_2$ in state $v_j$''. A state is accepting if \emph{both} components are in accepting states (intersection semantics).

Thus,

\[
L(M_1) \cap L(M_2) = L(M) = \{x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains both } 1 \text{ and } 0\}.
\]

\textbf{Verification:}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\varepsilon$: Neither contains $1$ nor $0$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $1$: Contains $1$ but not $0$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $0$: Contains $0$ but not $1$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $10$: Contains both $1$ and $0$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $01$: Contains both $1$ and $0$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $1100$: Contains both $1$ and $0$ $\to$ accepted 
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{Complement}

Formally,

\[L(M)^c = \Sigma^* \setminus L(M) = \{x \in \Sigma^* \mid x \notin L(M)\}\]

Given $M = (W, \Sigma, V, S, F)$, the complement is constructed as:

\[M^c = (W, \Sigma, V, S, F^c)\]

where

\[F^c = W \setminus F\]

The complement DFA has the same structure but with accepting and non-accepting states swapped.

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that contain at least one $1$.
The complement language is:

\[
L(M_1)^c = \{ x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains no } 1 \}
        = 0^*.
\]


Original DFA $M_1$: Accepts strings with at least one $1$.

$M_1 = (W,\Sigma,V,S,F)$ where

\[
W = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S = w_0, \quad
F = \{w_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};

    \path[->]
        (w0) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1)
        (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}


Complement Construction:

\[
M_1^c = (W,\Sigma,V,S,F^c)
\]

where

\[
F^c = W \setminus F = \{w_0,w_1\} \setminus \{w_1\} = \{w_0\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial, accepting] (w0c) {$w_0$};
    \node[state] (w1c) [right=of w0c] {$w_1$};

    \path[->]
        (w0c) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1c)
        (w0c) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1c) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

The complement DFA accepts all strings that the original rejects, and vice versa. State $w_0$ (previously non-accepting) is now accepting, while state $w_1$ (previously accepting) is now non-accepting.

Thus,

\[
L(M_1)^c = L(M_1^c) = 0^* = \{\varepsilon, 0, 00, 000, \ldots\}.
\]

Verification:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\varepsilon$: No $1$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $1$: Contains $1$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $10$: Contains $1$ $\to$ rejected
    \item $0$: No $1$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $000$: No $1$ $\to$ accepted 
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{Difference}

Formally,

\[L(M_1) \setminus L(M_2) = \{x \in \Sigma^* \mid x \in L(M_1) \land x \notin L(M_2)\}\]

The difference is constructed using intersection with complement:

\[L(M_1) \setminus L(M_2) = L(M_1) \cap L(M_2)^c\]

Given $M_1 = (W_1, \Sigma, V_1, S_1, F_1)$ and $M_2 = (W_2, \Sigma, V_2, S_2, F_2)$:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item First, construct $M_2^c = (W_2, \Sigma, V_2, S_2, F_2^c)$ where $F_2^c = W_2 \setminus F_2$
    \item Then, construct the product: $M = (W_1 \times W_2, \Sigma, V, (S_1, S_2), F)$ where
    
    \[F = \{(w_1, w_2) \mid w_1 \in F_1 \land w_2 \in F_2^c\}\]

    \item The transition function $V: (W_1 \times W_2) \times \Sigma \to (W_1 \times W_2)$ is defined as:

    \[
    V((w_i, v_j), x) = (V_1(w_i, x), V_2(v_j, x))
    \]

    for all $(w_i, v_j) \in W_1 \times W_2$ and $x \in \Sigma$.

\end{enumerate}


\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that contain at least one $1$,  
and let $L(M_2)$ be the set of strings that contain at least one $0$.

The difference language is:

\[
L(M_1) \setminus L(M_2)
  = \{ x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains at least one } 1 \text{ and contains no } 0 \}
  = 1^+.
\]

\textbf{DFA} $M_1$: Accepts strings with at least one $1$.

$M_1 = (W_1,\Sigma,V_1,S_1,F_1)$ where

\[
W_1 = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_1 = w_0, \quad
F_1 = \{w_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};

    \path[->]
        (w0) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1)
        (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{DFA} $M_2$: Accepts strings with at least one $0$.

$M_2 = (W_2,\Sigma,V_2,S_2,F_2)$ where

\[
W_2 = \{v_0,v_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_2 = v_0, \quad
F_2 = \{v_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (v0) {$v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (v1) [right=of v0] {$v_1$};

    \path[->]
        (v0) edge[bend right] node {0} (v1)
        (v0) edge[loop above] node {1} ()
        (v1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}


Difference Construction:

First, construct the complement of $M_2$:

\[
M_2^c = (W_2,\Sigma,V_2,S_2,F_2^c)
\quad\text{where}\quad
F_2^c = W_2 \setminus F_2 = \{v_0,v_1\} \setminus \{v_1\} = \{v_0\}.
\]

Then construct the product automaton:

\[
M = M_1 \setminus M_2 = (W_1 \times W_2,\ \Sigma,\ V,\ (w_0,v_0),\ F)
\]

where

\[
W = \{(w_0,v_0), (w_0,v_1), (w_1,v_0), (w_1,v_1)\}
\]

and

\[
F = \{(x,y) \mid x \in F_1 \land y \in F_2^c\} = \{(w_1,v_0)\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.8cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (00) {$w_0,v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (10) [right=of 00] {$w_1,v_0$};
    \node[state] (01) [below=of 00] {$w_0,v_1$};
    \node[state] (11) [right=of 01] {$w_1,v_1$};

    \path[->]
        (00) edge node[above] {1} (10)
        (00) edge node[left] {0} (01)

        (10) edge[loop right] node {1} ()
        (10) edge node[right] {0} (11)

        (01) edge node[below] {1} (11)
        (01) edge[loop left] node {0} ()

        (11) edge[loop right] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

The product state $(w_i, v_j)$ represents ``$M_1$ in state $w_i$ and $M_2^c$ in state $v_j$". Only state $(w_1, v_0)$ is accepting because it represents strings where $M_1$ accepts (contains $1$) AND $M_2^c$ accepts (contains no $0$).

Thus,

\[
L(M_1) \setminus L(M_2) = L(M) = 1^+ = \{1, 11, 111, \ldots\}.
\]

Verification:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\varepsilon$: No $1$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $1$: Contains $1$, no $0$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $0$: Contains $0$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $10$: Contains $0$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $111$: Contains $1$, no $0$ $\to$ accepted 
\end{enumerate}

\subsubsection{Concatenation}

Formally,

\[L(M_1) \cdot L(M_2) = \{xy \mid x \in L(M_1) \land y \in L(M_2)\}\]

Given DFAs $M_1 = (W_1, \Sigma, V_1, S_1, F_1)$ and $M_2 = (W_2, \Sigma, V_2, S_2, F_2)$, concatenation requires constructing an NFA (with $\varepsilon$-transitions):

\[M = M_1 \cdot M_2 = (W_1 \cup W_2,\ \Sigma,\ V,\ S_1,\ F_2)\]

where the transition function $V$ includes:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item All transitions from $M_1$: $V(w, x) = V_1(w, x)$ for $w \in W_1$
    \item All transitions from $M_2$: $V(w, x) = V_2(w, x)$ for $w \in W_2$
    \item $\varepsilon$-transitions from every accepting state of $M_1$ to the start state of $M_2$:
    \[V(f, \varepsilon) = S_2 \text{ for all } f \in F_1\]
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that contain at least one $1$,  
and let $L(M_2)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that contain at least one $0$.

The concatenation language is:

\[
L(M_1) \cdot L(M_2)
 = \{ uv \mid u \in L(M_1),\ v \in L(M_2) \}
\]

This represents all strings that can be split into two parts: the first part contains at least one $1$, and the second part contains at least one $0$.

\textbf{DFA} $M_1$: Accepts strings with at least one $1$.

$M_1 = (W_1,\Sigma,V_1,S_1,F_1)$ where

\[
W_1 = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_1 = w_0, \quad
F_1 = \{w_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};

    \path[->]
        (w0) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1)
        (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{DFA} $M_2$: Accepts strings with at least one $0$.

$M_2 = (W_2,\Sigma,V_2,S_2,F_2)$ where

\[
W_2 = \{v_0,v_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_2 = v_0, \quad
F_2 = \{v_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (v0) {$v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (v1) [right=of v0] {$v_1$};

    \path[->]
        (v0) edge[bend right] node {0} (v1)
        (v0) edge[loop above] node {1} ()
        (v1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Concatenation Construction (NFA):}

\[
M = M_1 \cdot M_2 = (W_1 \cup W_2,\ \Sigma,\ V,\ w_0,\ F_2)
\]

where $W_1 \cup W_2 = \{w_0, w_1, v_0, v_1\}$, start state is $w_0$, accepting states are $F = \{v_1\}$, and the $\varepsilon$-transition is:
\[
w_1 \xrightarrow{\varepsilon} v_0
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.9cm, on grid, auto]

    
    \node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$};
    \node[state] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};

    
    \node[state] (v0) [right=of w1] {$v_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (v1) [right=of v0] {$v_1$};

    
    \path[->]
        (w0) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1)
        (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

    
    \path[->]
        (w1) edge[dashed] node {$\varepsilon$} (v0);

    
    \path[->]
        (v0) edge[bend right] node {0} (v1)
        (v0) edge[loop above] node {1} ()
        (v1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

The $\varepsilon$-transition allows the NFA to non-deterministically ``jump'' from the accepting state of $M_1$ to the start state of $M_2$, effectively concatenating the two languages.

Thus,
\[
L(M_1 \cdot M_2) = L(M_1)L(M_2).
\]

\textbf{Verification:}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $10$: First part $1 \in L(M_1)$, second part $0 \in L(M_2)$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $110$: First part $11 \in L(M_1)$, second part $0 \in L(M_2)$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $100$: First part $1 \in L(M_1)$, second part $00 \in L(M_2)$ $\to$ accepted 
    \item $1100$: Multiple valid splits $\to$ accepted 
    \item $1$: No part with $0$ $\to$ rejected 
    \item $0$: No part with $1$ $\to$ rejected 
\end{enumerate}

Unlike the previous closure properties (union, intersection, complement, difference), concatenation requires an NFA with $\varepsilon$-transitions. DFAs alone cannot directly construct concatenation, though the resulting NFA can be converted to a DFA using standard NFA-to-DFA conversion.

\subsubsection{Kleene Star}

Formally,

\[L(M)^* = \{ w_1 w_2 \cdots w_k \mid k \ge 0,\ \forall i,\ w_i \in L(M) \}\]

Given a DFA $M = (W, \Sigma, V, S, F)$, the Kleene star requires constructing an NFA with $\varepsilon$-transitions:

\[M^* = (W \cup \{s\},\ \Sigma,\ V',\ s,\ F \cup \{s\})\]

where $s$ is a new start state (also accepting to include $\varepsilon$), and the transition function $V'$ includes:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item All transitions from $M$: $V'(w, x) = V(w, x)$ for $w \in W$
    \item $\varepsilon$-transition from the new start state to the original start state:
    \[V'(s, \varepsilon) = S\]
    \item $\varepsilon$-transitions from every accepting state back to the original start state:
    \[V'(f, \varepsilon) = S \text{ for all } f \in F\]
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that contain at least one $1$.

The Kleene star language is:

\[
L(M_1)^* = \{ w_1 w_2 \cdots w_k \mid k \ge 0,\ \forall i,\ w_i \in L(M_1) \}.
\]

Since each $w_i$ must contain at least one $1$, any string in $L(M_1)^*$ is a concatenation of blocks each containing a $1$.

\[
L(M_1)^* = \{0,1\}^* \setminus \{0^n \mid n \ge 1\}.
\]

\textbf{DFA} $M_1$: Accepts strings with at least one $1$.

$M_1 = (W_1,\Sigma,V_1,S_1,F_1)$ where

\[
W_1 = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_1 = w_0, \quad
F_1 = \{w_1\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]

    \node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};

    \path[->]
        (w0) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1)
        (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Kleene Star Construction (NFA):}

To build $M_1^*$, we create a new start/accepting state $s$ and add:

\[
s \xrightarrow{\varepsilon} S_1,\qquad
F_1 \xrightarrow{\varepsilon} S_1.
\]

So the NFA is:
\[
M_1^* = (W_1 \cup \{s\},\ \Sigma,\ V,\ s,\ F_1 \cup \{s\}).
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.4cm, on grid, auto]

    
    \node[state, initial, accepting] (s) {$s$};

    
    \node[state] (w0c) [right=of s] {$w_0$};
    \node[state, accepting] (w1c) [right=of w0c] {$w_1$};

    
    \path[->]
        (w0c) edge[bend left] node {1} (w1c)
        (w0c) edge[loop above] node {0} ()
        (w1c) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

    
    \path[->]
        (s) edge node {$\varepsilon$} (w0c)
        (w1c) edge[bend left] node {$\varepsilon$} (w0c);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

The new state $s$ is both initial and accepting (to accept $\varepsilon$). The $\varepsilon$-transition from $s$ to $w_0$ allows entry into $M_1$, and the $\varepsilon$-transition from $w_1$ back to $w_0$ allows repeated concatenation of strings from $L(M_1)$.

Thus,

\[
L(M_1)^* = L(M_1^*) = \{0,1\}^* \setminus \{0^n \mid n \ge 1\}.
\]

\textbf{Verification:}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\varepsilon$: Zero concatenations $(k=0)$ $\to$ accepted
    \item $1$: One block containing $1$ $\to$ accepted
    \item $11$: One block $11$ or two blocks $1 \cdot 1$ $\to$ accepted
    \item $101$: One block $101$ or two blocks $1 \cdot 01$ $\to$ accepted
    \item $0$: Single $0$ has no $1$ $\to$ rejected
    \item $00$: String $00$ cannot be split into blocks with $1$ $\to$ rejected
\end{enumerate}

Like concatenation, Kleene closure requires an NFA with $\varepsilon$-transitions. The resulting NFA can be converted to a DFA using standard NFA-to-DFA conversion.

\subsubsection{Reversal}

Formally,

\[L(M)^R = \{ x^R \mid x \in L(M) \}\]

where $x^R$ denotes the reverse of string $x$.

Given a DFA $M = (W, \Sigma, V, S, F)$, the reversal requires constructing an NFA:

\[M^R = (W,\ \Sigma,\ V^R,\ F,\ \{S\})\]

where the construction follows these steps:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Reverse all transitions: if $V(w_i, x) = w_j$ then $V^R(w_j, x) = w_i$
    \item Make original final states into the new start states: start states are $F$
    \item Make the original start state the only accepting state: $\{S\}$
    \item If $|F| > 1$, add a new initial state with $\varepsilon$-transitions to all states in $F$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that end with symbol $1$.

The reversal language is:

\[
L(M_1)^R = \{ x^R \mid x \in L(M_1) \}.
\]

Since $x$ ends with $1$, the reversed string $x^R$ must begin with $1$.

Thus,

\[
L(M_1)^R = \{1\{0,1\}^*\}.
\]

\textbf{DFA} $M_1$: Accepts strings ending with $1$.

$M_1 = (W_1,\Sigma,V_1,S_1,F_1)$ where

\[
W_1 = \{w_0,w_1,w_2\}, \quad
\Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad
S_1 = w_0, \quad
F_1 = \{w_2\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, on grid, auto]

    
    \node[state, initial]         (w0) at (0,0)   {$w_0$};
    \node[state]                  (w1) at (3,0)   {$w_1$};
    \node[state, accepting]       (w2) at (6,0)   {$w_2$};

    \path[->]
        (w0) edge node {0} (w1)
        (w0) edge[bend right=30] node[below] {1} (w2)

        (w1) edge node[above] {1} (w2)
        (w1) edge[loop above] node {0} ()

        (w2) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Reversal Construction (NFA):}

To construct $M_1^R$:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Reverse all transitions.
    \item Make original final states into the new start states.
    \item Make the original start state the only accepting state.
    \item Because multiple $\varepsilon$-entries may arise, the construction yields an NFA.
\end{enumerate}

Hence,

\[
M_1^R = (W_1,\ \Sigma,\ V^R,\ F_1,\ \{S_1\}).
\]

where $V^R$ is the reverse of $V_1$.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, on grid, auto]

    
    \node[state, accepting]  (r0) at (0,0)   {$w_0$};
    \node[state]             (r1) at (3,1.5) {$w_1$};
    \node[state, initial right]    (r2) at (6,0)   {$w_2$};

    \path[->]
        
        (r2) edge node[above] {1} (r1)
        (r2) edge[bend left=30] node[below] {1} (r0)
        (r2) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ()

        
        (r1) edge node[above] {0} (r0)
        (r1) edge[loop above] node {0} ();

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

The reversed automaton starts from the original accepting state $w_2$ and accepts at the original start state $w_0$. All transitions are reversed: edges that went from $w_i$ to $w_j$ on symbol $a$ now go from $w_j$ to $w_i$ on symbol $a$.

Therefore,

\[
L(M_1)^R = L(M_1^R) = \{1\{0,1\}^*\}.
\]

\textbf{Verification:}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Original: $1 \in L(M_1)$ (ends with $1$) $\to$ Reversed: $1 \in L(M_1)^R$ (begins with $1$) 
    \item Original: $01 \in L(M_1)$ $\to$ Reversed: $10 \in L(M_1)^R$ 
    \item Original: $101 \in L(M_1)$ $\to$ Reversed: $101 \in L(M_1)^R$ 
    \item Original: $0 \notin L(M_1)$ (ends with $0$) $\to$ Reversed: $0 \notin L(M_1)^R$ (begins with $0$) 
    \item Original: $10 \notin L(M_1)$ $\to$ Reversed: $01 \notin L(M_1)^R$ 
\end{enumerate}

Like concatenation and Kleene closure, reversal may require an NFA construction, especially when the original DFA has multiple accepting states.

\subsubsection{Homomorphism}

Formally,

A homomorphism $h: \Sigma^* \to \Gamma^*$ is a function that maps strings over alphabet $\Sigma$ to strings over alphabet $\Gamma$, defined by specifying $h(x)$ for each symbol $x \in \Sigma$ and extending to strings by concatenation:

\[h(\varepsilon) = \varepsilon, \quad h(xy) = h(x)h(y)\]

For a language $L \subseteq \Sigma^*$, the homomorphic image is:

\[h(L) = \{ h(x) \mid x \in L \}\]

Given a DFA $M = (W, \Sigma, V, S, F)$ accepting $L$, we can construct a DFA $M_h$ that accepts $h(L)$ by replacing each transition on symbol $a$ with a path corresponding to $h(a)$.

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_1)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that contain at least one $1$:

\[ L(M_1) = \{ x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains at least one } 1 \}. \]

Define a homomorphism $h: \{0,1\}^* \to \{0,1\}^*$:

\[ h(0) = 01, \quad h(1) = 1. \]

Then the homomorphic image of $L(M_1)$ is:

\[ h(L(M_1)) = \{ h(x) \mid x \in L(M_1) \}. \]

\textbf{DFA} $M_1$: Accepts strings with at least one $1$.

$M_1 = (W_1, \Sigma, V_1, S_1, F_1)$ where

\[ W_1 = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad \Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad S_1 = w_0, \quad F_1 = \{w_1\}. \]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]
\node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$}; 
\node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};
\path[->] 
    (w0) edge[bend left] node[above] {1} (w1) 
    (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} () 
    (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Homomorphism Construction:}

To construct a DFA for $h(L(M_1))$, we replace each transition:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Transition on $0$ becomes a path reading $h(0) = 01$
    \item Transition on $1$ becomes a path reading $h(1) = 1$
\end{enumerate}

The resulting DFA $M_h$ has additional intermediate states to read the multi-symbol strings $h(0)$ and $h(1)$.

\textbf{Applying the Homomorphism:}

The homomorphism $h$ transforms each $0$ into $01$, and each $1$ into $1$. For example:

\begin{align*}
x &= 0101 \in L(M_1) \\
h(x) &= h(0) \cdot h(1) \cdot h(0) \cdot h(1) \\
&= 01 \cdot 1 \cdot 01 \cdot 1 \\
&= 011011 \in h(L(M_1))
\end{align*}

\textbf{Verification:}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $x = 1 \in L(M_1) \to h(1) = 1 \in h(L(M_1))$
    \item $x = 0 \notin L(M_1) \to h(0) = 01 \notin h(L(M_1))$
    \item $x = 10 \in L(M_1) \to h(10) = h(1)h(0) = 1 \cdot 01 = 101 \in h(L(M_1))$
    \item $x = 01 \in L(M_1) \to h(01) = h(0)h(1) = 01 \cdot 1 = 011 \in h(L(M_1))$
    \item $x = 101 \in L(M_1) \to h(101) = 1 \cdot 01 \cdot 1 = 1011 \in h(L(M_1))$
\end{enumerate}

Therefore,

\[ h(L(M_1)) \text{ is regular.} \]

Regular languages are closed under homomorphism: if $L$ is regular, then $h(L)$ is also regular for any homomorphism $h$.

\subsubsection{Inverse Homomorphism}

Formally,

Given a homomorphism $h: \Sigma^* \to \Gamma^*$ and a language $L \subseteq \Gamma^*$, the inverse homomorphic image is:

\[h^{-1}(L) = \{ x \in \Sigma^* \mid h(x) \in L \}\]

Given a DFA $M = (W, \Gamma, V, S, F)$ accepting $L$, we can construct a DFA $M_{h^{-1}}$ that accepts $h^{-1}(L)$ by simulating $M$ on $h(x)$ for each input symbol $x \in \Sigma$.
The construction works as follows: for each state $w$ in $M$ and each symbol $x \in \Sigma$, we determine where $M$ goes when reading the string $h(x)$, and use that as the transition in $M_{h^{-1}}$.

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_2)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that start with $1$:

\[ L(M_2) = \{ x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ starts with } 1 \}. \]

Define a homomorphism $h: \{0,1\}^* \to \{0,1\}^*$:

\[ h(0) = 01, \quad h(1) = 1. \]

The inverse homomorphic image of $L(M_2)$ is:

\[ h^{-1}(L(M_2)) = \{x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid h(x) \in L(M_2) \}. \]

\textbf{DFA} $M_2$: Accepts strings starting with $1$.

$M_2 = (W_2, \Sigma, V_2, S_2, F_2)$ where

\[ W_2 = \{w_0,w_1\}, \quad \Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad S_2 = w_0, \quad F_2 = \{w_1\}. \]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]
\node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$}; 
\node[state, accepting] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$};
\path[->] 
    (w0) edge[bend left] node[above] {1} (w1) 
    (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} () 
    (w1) edge[loop above] node {0,1} ();
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Inverse Homomorphism Construction:}

To construct a DFA for $h^{-1}(L(M_2))$, for each state $w$ and input symbol $x$, we compute where $M_2$ goes when reading $h(x)$:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item From $w_0$ on input $0$: $M_2$ reads $h(0) = 01$, which goes $w_0 \xrightarrow{0} w_0 \xrightarrow{1} w_1$
    \item From $w_0$ on input $1$: $M_2$ reads $h(1) = 1$, which goes $w_0 \xrightarrow{1} w_1$
    \item From $w_1$ on input $0$: $M_2$ reads $h(0) = 01$, which goes $w_1 \xrightarrow{0} w_1 \xrightarrow{1} w_1$
    \item From $w_1$ on input $1$: $M_2$ reads $h(1) = 1$, which goes $w_1 \xrightarrow{1} w_1$
\end{enumerate}

This gives us the transitions for $M_{h^{-1}}$.

\textbf{Applying the Inverse Homomorphism:}

The inverse homomorphism $h^{-1}$ gives the set of strings $x$ such that $h(x)$ starts with $1$. For instance:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $x = 0 \to h(0) = 01$ (starts with $0$) $\to 0 \notin h^{-1}(L(M_2))$
    \item $x = 1 \to h(1) = 1$ (starts with $1$) $\to 1 \in h^{-1}(L(M_2))$
    \item $x = 00 \to h(00) = 01 \cdot 01 = 0101$ (starts with $0$) $\to 00 \notin h^{-1}(L(M_2))$
    \item $x = 10 \to h(10) = 1 \cdot 01 = 101$ (starts with $1$) $\to 10 \in h^{-1}(L(M_2))$
    \item $x = 01 \to h(01) = 01 \cdot 1 = 011$ (starts with $0$) $\to 01 \notin h^{-1}(L(M_2))$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Verification:}

Notice that $h^{-1}(L(M_2))$ accepts strings where:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item The first symbol is $1$ (since $h(1) = 1$ starts with $1$), or
    \item After any number of $0$s, the first $1$ appears (since reading $0$s produces strings starting with $0$, but eventually a $1$ will make $h(x)$ start with $1$)
\end{enumerate}

Therefore,

\[ h^{-1}(L(M_2)) = \{x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains at least one } 1\} \]

which is regular.

Regular languages are closed under inverse homomorphism: if $L$ is regular, then $h^{-1}(L)$ is also regular for any homomorphism $h$.

\subsubsection{Substitution}

Formally,

A substitution $\psi$ is a function that maps each symbol $x \in \Sigma$ to a language $\psi(x) \subseteq \Gamma^*$. It extends to strings by:

\[\psi(\varepsilon) = \{\varepsilon\}, \quad \psi(wx) = \psi(w) \cdot \psi(x)\]

where $L_1 \cdot L_2 = \{xy \mid x \in L_1, y \in L_2\}$ is the concatenation of languages.

For a language $L \subseteq \Sigma^*$, the substitution is:

\[\psi(L) = \bigcup_{w \in L} \psi(w)\]

If $L$ is regular and each $\psi(x)$ is regular, then $\psi(L)$ is also regular (regular languages are closed under substitution).

\textbf{Example:}

Let $L(M_3)$ be the set of strings over $\{0,1\}$ that contain exactly one $1$:

\[ L(M_3) = \{x \in \{0,1\}^* \mid x \text{ contains exactly one } 1 \}. \]

Define a substitution $\psi: \{0,1\}^* \to \{0,1\}^*$:

\[ \psi(0) = \{0,00\}, \quad \psi(1) = \{1,11\}. \]

The substitution of $L(M_3)$ is:

\[ \psi(L(M_3)) = \bigcup_{x \in L(M_3)} \psi(x), \]

where $\psi(x)$ replaces each symbol $y$ in $x$ by a string from $\psi(y)$.

\textbf{DFA} $M_3$: Accepts strings with exactly one $1$.

$M_3 = (W_3, \Sigma, V_3, S_3, F_3)$ where

\[ W_3 = \{w_0,w_1,w_2\}, \quad \Sigma = \{0,1\}, \quad S_3 = w_0, \quad F_3 = \{w_2\}. \]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[shorten >=1pt, node distance=2.6cm, on grid, auto]
\node[state, initial] (w0) {$w_0$}; 
\node[state] (w1) [right=of w0] {$w_1$}; 
\node[state, accepting] (w2) [right=of w1] {$w_2$};
\path[->] 
    (w0) edge node {1} (w1) 
    (w0) edge[loop above] node {0} () 
    (w1) edge node {0} (w2) 
    (w2) edge[loop above] node {0} ();
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\textbf{Applying the Substitution:}

The substitution allows each $0$ to be replaced by $0$ or $00$, and each $1$ by $1$ or $11$. For example:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $x = 1 \in L(M_3)$:
    \[\psi(1) = \{1, 11\}\]
    
    \item $x = 01 \in L(M_3)$:
    \[\psi(01) = \psi(0) \cdot \psi(1) = \{0, 00\} \cdot \{1, 11\} = \{01, 011, 001, 0011\}\]
    
    \item $x = 10 \in L(M_3)$:
    \[\psi(10) = \psi(1) \cdot \psi(0) = \{1, 11\} \cdot \{0, 00\} = \{10, 100, 110, 1100\}\]
    
    \item $x = 010 \in L(M_3)$:
    \[\psi(010) = \psi(0) \cdot \psi(1) \cdot \psi(0) = \{0, 00\} \cdot \{1, 11\} \cdot \{0, 00\}\]
    \[= \{010, 0100, 0110, 01100, 0010, 00100, 00110, 001100\}\]
\end{enumerate}

Therefore,

\[ \psi(L(M_3)) = \bigcup_{w \in L(M_3)} \psi(w) \]

is the union of all possible ways to substitute symbols in strings from $L(M_3)$.

Since $L(M_3)$ is regular and both $\psi(0) = \{0, 00\}$ and $\psi(1) = \{1, 11\}$ are regular languages (both can be accepted by simple DFAs), we conclude:

\[ \psi(L(M_3)) \text{ is regular.} \]

Regular languages are closed under substitution when each $\psi(a)$ is a regular language.

\section{Pumping Lemma}

For any regular language $L$, there exists a constant $p \geq 1$ (called the pumping length) such that for any string $\phi \in L$ with $|\phi| \geq p$, there exists a decomposition $\phi = xyz$ satisfying:

\[|y| > 0, \ |xy| \leq p, \ \forall i \geq 0, \ xy^iz \in L\]


\textbf{Example:}

Let 

\[\aleph = \{0^n 1^n \mid n \geq 0\} \ \text{is not regular}\]

\emph{Proof}

Assume $\aleph$ is regular. Let $p$ be the pumping length. 
Consider $\phi = 0^p 1^p \in \aleph$ with $|\phi| = 2p \geq p$. 
By the Pumping Lemma, $\phi = xyz$ where $|y| > 0$, $|xy| \leq p$, and $\forall i \geq 0, xy^iz \in \aleph$. 
Since $|xy| \leq p$, both $x$ and $y$ consist only of $0$'s. Let $y = 0^k$ where $k > 0$. 
Pump with $i = 2$: $xy^2z = 0^{p+k}1^p$. Since $k > 0$, we have $p + k > p$, so $xy^2z \notin \aleph$. Contradiction. Therefore, $\aleph$ is not regular. 

\hfill $QED$

\textbf{Intuitively:}

Suppose $p = 7$. Consider $\phi = 0^71^7$ where $|xy| \leq 7$. There are three cases for where $y$ can be located:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $y$ is in the $0$ part.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{00}_x \underbrace{0000}_y \underbrace{01^7}_z$.

    Pumping with $i = 2$: $xy^2z = 0^{11}1^7 \notin \aleph$ since $11 \neq 7$. 
    
    
    \item $y$ is in the $1$ part.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{0^7}_x \underbrace{11}_y \underbrace{1^5}_z$.
    
    Pumping with $i = 2$: $xy^2z = 0^71^{9} \notin \aleph$ since $7 \neq 9$. 
    
    
    \item $y$ spans both $0$ and $1$ parts.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{0000}_x \underbrace{0001}_y \underbrace{1^6}_z$.
    
    Pumping with $i = 2$: $xy^2z = 000000001001 1^6 \notin \aleph$ (wrong pattern for $0^n1^n$). 
\end{enumerate}

Since $|xy| \leq p = 7$, all cases lead to contradiction. Therefore, $\aleph$ is not regular.

\section{Regular Grammar}

Formally

\[
G = (N, T, S, R)
\]

where:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $N$ = a finite set of variables (nonterminal symbols),
    \item $T$ = a finite set of terminal symbols,
    \item $S$ = the start symbol $(S \in N)$,
    \item $R$ = the set of production rules for terminals and nonterminals.
\end{enumerate}


\textbf{In general:}

Let 

\[
G = (\{f, g, h\},\ \{0,1\},\ f,\ \{f \to gh,\ g \to 0,\ h \to 1\})
\]

where

\begin{align*}
N &= \{f, g, h\} \qquad & T &= \{0, 1\},\\
S &= f               \qquad & R &= \{\,f \to gh,\ g \to 0,\ h \to 1\,\}.
\end{align*}


From this we can derive 

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to gh \\
  &\to 0h \\
  &\to 01
\end{aligned}
\]

Hence, we can coclude, \[\fbox{L(G) = \{01\}}\]

\section{Context-Free Grammar and Language}

Formally, a context-free grammar is \(G=(N,\Sigma,R,S)\) where

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $N$ is the set of nonterminals.
    \item $\Sigma$ is the set of terminals.
    \item $S \in N$ is the start symbol.
    \item $R \subseteq N \times (N \cup \Sigma)^*$ is the set of productions, each of the form
    $A \to \alpha$ with $A \in N$ and $\alpha \in (N \cup \Sigma)^*$.
\end{enumerate}

To produce strings, a CFG uses the operations of concatenation and union.

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Concatenation: If $A \to \alpha$ and $B \to \beta$, then writing $\alpha\beta$
    denotes placing the two strings next to each other.

    \item Union: A production such as $A \to xA \mid x$ means 
    $A$ may produce either $xA$ or $x$.
\end{enumerate}


\textbf{Example:}

Consider the grammar

\[
G = ( \{f,g,h\}, \{x,y\}, f,\ 
   \{ f \to gh,\ g \to xg \mid x,\ h \to yh \mid y \} ).
\]

This grammar generates strings consisting of one or more $x$'s followed by 
one or more $y$'s. 

\noindent
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.48\textwidth}
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to \underline{g}h & [f \to gh] \\
              &\to xh               & [g \to x] \\
              &\to xy               & [h \to y]
\end{aligned}
\]

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to \underline{g}h  & [f \to gh] \\
              &\to x\underline{g}h & [g \to xg] \\
              &\to xxh             & [g \to x] \\
              &\to xxy             & [h \to y]
\end{aligned}
\]
\end{minipage}
\hfill
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.48\textwidth}
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to g\underline{h}  & [f \to gh] \\
              &\to gy\underline{h} & [h \to yh] \\ 
              &\to gyy             & [h \to y] \\
              &\to xyy             & [g \to x]
\end{aligned}
\]

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to \underline{g}h   & [f \to gh] \\
              &\to x\underline{g}h  & [g \to xg] \\
              &\to xx\underline{h}  & [g \to x] \\
              &\to xxy\underline{h} & [h \to yh] \\
              &\to xxyy             & [h \to y]
\end{aligned}
\]
\end{minipage}

Therefore,

\[
\fbox{$L(G) = \{ xy, x^2y, xy^2, x^2y^2, \dots \}$}
\]

More compactly,

\[
\fbox{$L(G) = \{ x^m y^n \mid m \ge 1,\ n \ge 1 \}$}.
\]


\subsection{Recursive}

Formally, a nonterminal $A \in V$ is called \emph{recursive} if there exists a derivation

\[
A \to^{+} \alpha A \beta
\]

for some $\alpha, \beta \in (N \cup \Sigma)^*$.

\begin{enumerate}
    

\item \textbf{Example 1:}

Let the grammar

\[
G = (\{f\},\ \{0,1\},\ f,\ \{\, f \to 01,\quad f \to 0f1 \,\})
\]

We can derive:

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to 0\underline{f}1 & [f \to 0f1] \\
              &\to 0011            & [f \to 01]
\end{aligned}
\]

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to 0\underline{f}1 & [f \to 0f1] \\
              &\to 00\underline{f}11 & [f \to 0f1] \\
              &\to 000111            & [f \to 01]
\end{aligned}
\]

Thus the language is

\[\boxed{L(G) = \{\, 0^n 1^n \mid n \ge 1 \,\}}\]


\item  \textbf{Example 2:}

Let

\[
G=(\{f,g\},\ \{0,1\},\ f,\ \{\, f\to 0g1,\quad g\to 0g1,\quad g\to\varepsilon \,\})
\]


Then we can derive:

\noindent
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.48\textwidth}
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to 0\underline{g}1 & [\text{by } f\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 0\underline{0g1}1 & [\text{by } g\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 00\underline{0g1}11 & [\text{by } g\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 000\underline{g}111 & [\text{by } g\to\varepsilon] \\
              &= 000111
\end{aligned}
\]
\end{minipage}
\hfill
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.48\textwidth}
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to 0\underline{g}1 & [\text{by } f\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 01 & [\text{by } g\to\varepsilon] \\
              &= 01
\end{aligned}
\]
\end{minipage}

\vspace{1em}

\noindent
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.48\textwidth}
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to 0\underline{g}1 & [\text{by } f\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 0\underline{0g1}1 & [\text{by } g\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 00\underline{g}11 & [\text{by } g\to\varepsilon] \\
              &= 0011
\end{aligned}
\]
\end{minipage}
\hfill
\begin{minipage}[t]{0.48\textwidth}
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to 0\underline{g}1 & [\text{by } f\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 0\underline{0g1}1 & [\text{by } g\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 00\underline{0g1}11 & [\text{by } g\to 0g1] \\
              &\to 0000\underline{g}1111 & [\text{by } g\to\varepsilon] \\
              &= 00001111
\end{aligned}
\]
\end{minipage}

This grammar generates

\[
\boxed{L(G)=\{0^n 1^n \mid n\ge 1\}}.
\]

Note that the grammar defines how to produce strings; the language is the complete set of strings producible from that grammar.

\item \textbf{Example 3:}


Let

\[
G = (\{f\},\ \{(,)\},\ f,\ \{\, f \to (f),\quad f \to ff,\quad f \to \varepsilon \,\})
\]

A sample derivation of \(()\):

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to (\underline{f})     & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to ((\underline{f}))   & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to (((\underline{f}))) & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to ((()))              & [f \to \varepsilon]
\end{aligned}
\]

A sample derivation of \(()()\):

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to \underline{f}f         & [f \to ff] \\
              &\to (\underline{f})f       & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to ()f                    & [f \to \varepsilon] \\
              &\to ()(\underline{f})      & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to ()()                   & [f \to \varepsilon]
\end{aligned}
\]

A sample derivation of \(((()()))\):

\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to (\underline{f})          & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to ((\underline{f}))        & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to ((\underline{f}f))       & [f \to ff] \\
              &\to ((()\underline{f}))      & [f \to (f)] \\
              &\to ((()()) )                & [f \to (f), \ \text{then} \ f \to \varepsilon] \\
              &\to ((()()))                 & [f \to \varepsilon]
\end{aligned}
\]

Therefore,

\[
\boxed{L(G)=\text{all balanced parentheses strings}}.
\]

\item \textbf{Example 4:}

Let

\[
G = (\{g\},\ \{+,*,(,),a\},\ g,\ \{\, g \to g + g,\quad g \to g * g,\quad g \to (g),\quad g \to a \,\})
\]

\noindent
A sample derivation of \((a+a)*a\):

\[
\begin{aligned}
g &\to \underline{g} * g        & [g \to g * g] \\
              &\to (\underline{g}) * g      & [g \to (g)] \\
              &\to (\underline{g} + g) * g  & [g \to g + g] \\
              &\to (a + \underline{g}) * g  & [g \to a] \\
              &\to (a + a) * \underline{g}  & [g \to a] \\
              &\to (a + a) * a              & [g \to a]
\end{aligned}
\]

Hence,

\[
\boxed{L(G)=\text{the set of all well-formed arithmetic expressions over } a}
\]

(with operations \(+,*,()\)).

\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Right and Left Linear Grammar}

\begin{enumerate}


\item Right Linear (nonterminal on the right):

\[
g \to 1h \quad \text{(produces strings like } 111\ldots \text{ from left to right)}
\]

\textbf{Example:}

Let

\[
G_R = (\{f, g\},\ \{1,0\},\ f,\ R_R)
\]

with productions

\[
R_R = \{\, f \to 1g,\ g \to 1g,\ g \to 0 \,\}.
\]

This generates strings such as

\[
f \to 1g \to 11g \to 111g \to 1110.
\]

\item Left Linear (nonterminal on the left):

\[
g \to h1 \quad \text{(produces strings like } \ldots 111 \text{ from right to left)}
\]

\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}

Let

\[
G_L = (\{f, g\},\ \{1,0\},\ f,\ R_L)
\]

with productions

\[
R_L = \{\, f \to g1,\ g \to g1,\ g \to 0 \,\}.
\]

This generates the same language, but the steps look reversed:

\[
f \to g1 \to g11 \to g111 \to 0111.
\]

\subsection{Pumping Lemma for Contex-Free Languages}

For any context-free language $L$, there exists a constant $p \geq 1$ such that for any string $\phi \in L$ with $|\phi| \geq p$, there exists a decomposition $\phi = uvxyz$ satisfying:

\[|vy| > 0, \ |vxy| \leq p, \ \forall i \geq 0, \ uv^ixy^iz \in L\]


\textbf{Example:}

Let 

\[\aleph = \{f^n g^n h^n \mid n \geq 0\} \ \text{is not context-free}\]

\emph{Proof}

Assume $\aleph$ is context-free. Let $p$ be the pumping length. 
Consider $\phi = f^p g^p h^p \in \aleph$ with $|\phi| = 3p \geq p$. 
By the Pumping Lemma, $\phi = uvxyz$ where $|vy| > 0$, $|vxy| \leq p$, and $\forall i \geq 0, \ uv^ixy^iz \in \aleph$. 
Since $|vxy| \leq p$ and $\phi$ contains $p$ copies each of $f$, $g$, and $h$, the substring $vxy$ can contain at most two distinct symbols. Therefore, $v$ and $y$ together can only pump at most two of the three symbols $f$, $g$, $h$. 
Pump with $i = 2$: $uv^2xy^2z$ will have unequal counts of $f$'s, $g$'s, and $h$'s. Thus $uv^2xy^2z \notin \aleph$. Contradiction. Therefore, $\aleph$ is not context-free.

\hfill $QED$

\textbf{Intuitively:}

Suppose $p = 4$. Consider $\phi = f^4g^4h^4$ where $|vxy| \leq 4$. There are several cases for where $vxy$ can be located:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $vxy$ is in the $f$ part only.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{}_u \underbrace{f}_v \underbrace{ff}_x \underbrace{f}_y \underbrace{g^4h^4}_z$.

    Pumping with $i = 2$: $uv^2xy^2z = f^{6}g^4h^4 \notin \aleph$ since $6 \neq 4 \neq 4$. 
    
    
    \item $vxy$ spans the $f$ and $g$ parts.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{ff}_u \underbrace{f}_v \underbrace{fg}_x \underbrace{g}_y \underbrace{g^2h^4}_z$.
    
    Pumping with $i = 2$: $uv^2xy^2z = f^{5}g^{5}h^4 \notin \aleph$ since $5 \neq 4$. 
    
    
    \item $vxy$ is in the $g$ part only.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{f^4}_u \underbrace{g}_v \underbrace{gg}_x \underbrace{g}_y \underbrace{h^4}_z$.
    
    Pumping with $i = 2$: $uv^2xy^2z = f^4g^{6}h^4 \notin \aleph$ since $4 \neq 6 \neq 4$. 
    
    \item $vxy$ spans the $g$ and $h$ parts.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{f^4g^2}_u \underbrace{g}_v \underbrace{gh}_x \underbrace{h}_y \underbrace{h^2}_z$.
    
    Pumping with $i = 2$: $uv^2xy^2z = f^4g^{5}h^{5} \notin \aleph$ since $4 \neq 5$. 
    
    \item $vxy$ is in the $h$ part only.
    
    Let $\phi = \underbrace{f^4g^4}_u \underbrace{h}_v \underbrace{hh}_x \underbrace{h}_y \underbrace{}_z$.
    
    Pumping with $i = 2$: $uv^2xy^2z = f^4g^4h^{6} \notin \aleph$ since $4 \neq 6$. 
\end{enumerate}

Since $|vxy| \leq p = 4$, the substring $vxy$ can span at most two of the three symbol types. In all cases, pumping causes unequal counts. Therefore, $\aleph$ is not context-free.

\section{Context-Sensitive Grammar (CSG)}

A \emph{context-sensitive grammar} (CSG) is a 4-tuple $G = (V, \Sigma, R, S)$ where:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $V$ is a finite set of variables (non-terminals),
    \item $\Sigma$ is a finite set of terminals, disjoint from $V$,
    \item $R$ is a finite set of production rules of the form $\alpha A \beta \to \alpha \gamma \beta$,
    where $A \in V$, $\alpha, \beta \in (V \cup \Sigma)^*$, and $\gamma \in (V \cup \Sigma)^+$,
    \item $S \in V$ is the start variable.
\end{enumerate}

Every rule $\alpha \to \beta \in R$ must satisfy $|\alpha| \leq |\beta|$ (non-contracting condition).

The language generated by $G$ is:

\[
L(G) = \{ w \in \Sigma^* \mid S \overset{*}{\to} w \}
\]


\textbf{Example:} 

Consider the language \[L = \{a^n b^n c^n \mid n \geq 1\}\]

Define $G = (V, \Sigma, R, S)$ where:

\[
V = \{S, B, C\}, \qquad \Sigma = \{a, b, c\}, \qquad S \text{ is the start variable}
\]

with production rules $R$:

\begin{align*}
S &\to aSBC \mid aBC \\
CB &\to BC \\
aB &\to ab \\
bB &\to bb \\
bC &\to bc \\
cC &\to cc
\end{align*}

Derivation of $aabbcc$:

\begin{align*}
S &\to aSBC \\
  &\to a \cdot aBC \cdot BC \\
  &\to aaBCBC \\
  &\to aaBBCC \quad (CB \to BC) \\
  &\to aabBCC \quad (aB \to ab) \\
  &\to aabbCC \quad (bB \to bb) \\
  &\to aabbcC \quad (bC \to bc) \\
  &\to aabbcc \quad (cC \to cc)
\end{align*}

\noindent This language cannot be generated by any context-free grammar, since the pumping lemma for CFLs is violated. However, the CSG above generates it correctly by using context to ensure the counts of $a$, $b$, and $c$ remain equal.

\section{Derivation Tree}

A Derivation Tree or Parse Tree is an ordered rooted tree that graphically represents
the semantic information of strings derived from a Context-free Grammar. 

\textbf{Example:}

Given the grammar:

\[G_T = \{f \to 0h, g \to 1gg \mid \varepsilon, h \to 0gg\}\]

Derivation tree for the string ``001":

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.5cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=4cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=2cm},
  level 3/.style={sibling distance=1cm},
]
  \node {f}
    child {node {0}}
    child {node {h}
      child {node {0}}
      child {node {g}
        child {node {1}}
        child {node {g}
          child {node {$\varepsilon$}}
        }
        child {node {g}
          child {node {$\varepsilon$}}
        }
      }
      child {node {g}
        child {node {$\varepsilon$}}
      }
    };
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\subsection{Right and Left Derivation}

\textbf{Example:}

Given the grammar:

\[G_T = \{f \to 1gf \mid 1ff \mid \varepsilon , g \to f0g \mid 01\}\]

Generate the string ``11011":


\begin{enumerate}

    \item Left derivation
    
    \begin{center}
    \fbox{
    \begin{tikzpicture}[
      level distance=1.3cm,
      level 1/.style={sibling distance=3cm},
      level 2/.style={sibling distance=2cm},
      level 3/.style={sibling distance=1.2cm},
      ]
      \node {f}
        child {node[red] {1}}
        child {node {f}
          child {node[red] {1}}
          child {node {g}
            child {node[red] {0}}
            child {node[red] {1}}
          }
          child {node {f}
            child {node[red] {1}}
          }
        }
        child {node {f}
          child {node {$\varepsilon$}}
        };
    \end{tikzpicture}
    }
    \end{center}

    \item Right derivation:
    
    \begin{center}
    \fbox{
    \begin{tikzpicture}[
      level distance=1.3cm,
      level 1/.style={sibling distance=2cm},
      level 2/.style={sibling distance=2.5cm},
      level 3/.style={sibling distance=1cm},
      level 4/.style={sibling distance=0.8cm},
      ]
      \node {f}
        child {node[red] {1}}
        child {node {f}
        }
        child {node {f}
          child {node[red] {1}}
          child {node {g}
            child {node[red] {0}}
            child {node[red] {1}}
          }
          child {node {f}
            child {node[red] {1}}
            child {node {f}
              child {node {$\varepsilon$}}
            }
            child {node {f}
              child {node {$\varepsilon$}}
            }
          }
        };
    \end{tikzpicture}
    }
    \end{center}

\end{enumerate}

\subsection{Ambiguous Grammar}

A grammar is said to be ambiguous if there exist two or more derivation tree for a string

\textbf{Example:}

Let 
\[G = (\{f\}, \{1,0, +, *\}, f, \{f \to f + f \mid f*f \mid 1 \mid 0\})\]

This grammar is ambiguous because we can derive the string $1 + (1 * 0)$ in two different ways:
\begin{enumerate}

\item Derivation 1 (Addition at root)
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to f + f \\
  &\to 1 + f \\
  &\to 1 + f * f \\
  &\to 1 + 1 * f \\
  &\to 1 + 1 * 0
\end{aligned}
\]

This corresponds to the parse tree: $1 + (1 * 0)$ where multiplication has higher precedence.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  level distance=1.5cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.5cm},
  level 3/.style={sibling distance=1cm}
]
\node {f}
  child {node {f}
    child {node {1}}
  }
  child {node {+}}
  child {node {f}
    child {node {f}
      child {node {1}}
    }
    child {node {*}}
    child {node {f}
      child {node {0}}
    }
  };
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}


\item Derivation 2 (Multiplication at root)
\[
\begin{aligned}
f &\to f * f \\
  &\to f + f * f \\
  &\to 1 + f * f \\
  &\to 1 + 1 * f \\
  &\to 1 + 1 * 0
\end{aligned}
\]

This corresponds to the parse tree: $(1 + 1) * 0$ where addition has higher precedence.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  level distance=1.5cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.5cm},
  level 3/.style={sibling distance=1cm}
]
\node {f}
  child {node {f}
    child {node {f}
      child {node {1}}
    }
    child {node {+}}
    child {node {f}
      child {node {1}}
    }
  }
  child {node {*}}
  child {node {f}
    child {node {0}}
  };
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\end{enumerate}

Since the same string $1 + (1 * 0)$ can be derived with two different parse trees (different structural interpretations), the grammar is ambiguous.
This ambiguity arises because the grammar doesn't specify operator precedence or associativity rules.

\section{Breadth-First Search (BFS)}

Breadth-First Search is a graph and tree traversal algorithm that explores
nodes level by level, visiting all nodes at the current depth before proceeding
to the next. It uses a queue (First-In First-Out) to determine the
order in which nodes are visited.

Formally,given a graph $G = (V, E)$ and a source node $s \in V$, BFS computes the
shortest path from $s$ to every reachable vertex. It maintains a queue $Q$ and
a visited set $\mathcal{V}$, and proceeds as follows:

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Initialize $Q \leftarrow \{s\}$ and $\mathcal{V} \leftarrow \{s\}$.
  \item While $Q \neq \varnothing$:
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Dequeue the front node $u$ from $Q$.
    \item For each neighbor $v \in \text{Adj}(u)$:
    \begin{enumerate}
      \item If $v \notin \mathcal{V}$, add $v$ to $\mathcal{V}$ and enqueue $v$.
    \end{enumerate}
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

The BFS tree rooted at $s$ consists of all edges $(u, v)$ traversed
during the search. For any vertex $v$ reachable from $s$, the path in the BFS
tree is the shortest path in $G$.

\textbf{Example:}

Consider the following graph with source node $w_1$:

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[->, >=stealth, node distance=2.5cm]

  \node[state] (w1) {$w_1$};
  \node[state] (w2) [above right of=w1] {$w_2$};
  \node[state] (w3) [below right of=w1] {$w_3$};
  \node[state] (w4) [right of=w2] {$w_4$};
  \node[state] (w5) [right of=w3] {$w_5$};
  \node[state] (w6) [below right of=w4] {$w_6$};

  \draw (w1) edge node[above] {} (w2);
  \draw (w1) edge node[below] {} (w3);
  \draw (w2) edge node[above] {} (w4);
  \draw (w3) edge node[below] {} (w5);
  \draw (w4) edge node[right] {} (w6);
  \draw (w5) edge node[right] {} (w6);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

Starting from $w_1$, BFS proceeds as follows:

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c}
\textbf{Step} & \textbf{Dequeued} & \textbf{Queue after step} \\
\hline
1 & w_1 & w_2,\; w_3 \\
2 & w_2 & w_3,\; w_4 \\
3 & w_3 & w_4,\; w_5 \\
4 & w_4 & w_5,\; w_6 \\
5 & w_5 & w_6 \\
6 & w_6 & \varnothing \\
\end{array}
\]

The BFS traversal order is therefore:
\[
w_1 \;\to\; w_2 \;\to\; w_3 \;\to\; w_4 \;\to\; w_5 \;\to\; w_6
\]

All nodes at depth 1 ($w_2$, $w_3$) are visited before any node at depth 2
($w_4$, $w_5$), which are visited before depth 3 ($w_6$). This level-by-level
guarantee ensures that BFS always finds the shallowest solution first.

\section{Depth-First Search (DFS)}

Depth-First Search is a graph and tree traversal algorithm that explores
nodes by going as deep as possible along each branch before backtracking.
It uses a stack (Last-In First-Out), or equivalently, recursion to
determine the order in which nodes are visited.

Formally, given a graph $G = (V, E)$ and a source node $s \in V$, DFS
explores all vertices reachable from $s$. It maintains a stack $S$ and
a visited set $\mathcal{V}$, and proceeds as follows:

\begin{enumerate}
  \item Initialize $S \leftarrow \{s\}$ and $\mathcal{V} \leftarrow \{s\}$.
  \item While $S \neq \varnothing$:
  \begin{enumerate}
    \item Pop the top node $u$ from $S$.
    \item For each neighbor $v \in \text{Adj}(u)$:
    \begin{enumerate}
      \item If $v \notin \mathcal{V}$, add $v$ to $\mathcal{V}$ and push $v$ onto $S$.
    \end{enumerate}
  \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

The DFS tree rooted at $s$ consists of all edges $(u, v)$ traversed
during the search. Edges in $G$ are classified with respect to the DFS tree as
\emph{tree edges}, \emph{back edges}, \emph{forward edges}, or \emph{cross edges}.
A graph $G$ is acyclic if and only if DFS produces no back edges.


\textbf{Example:}

Consider the following graph with source node $w_1$:

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[->, >=stealth, node distance=2.5cm]

  \node[state] (w1) {$w_1$};
  \node[state] (w2) [above right of=w1] {$w_2$};
  \node[state] (w3) [below right of=w1] {$w_3$};
  \node[state] (w4) [right of=w2] {$w_4$};
  \node[state] (w5) [right of=w3] {$w_5$};
  \node[state] (w6) [below right of=w4] {$w_6$};

  \draw (w1) edge node[above] {} (w2);
  \draw (w1) edge node[below] {} (w3);
  \draw (w2) edge node[above] {} (w4);
  \draw (w3) edge node[below] {} (w5);
  \draw (w4) edge node[right] {} (w6);
  \draw (w5) edge node[right] {} (w6);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

Starting from $w_1$, DFS proceeds as follows:

\[
\begin{array}{c|c|c}
\textbf{Step} & \textbf{Popped} & \textbf{Stack after step} \\
\hline
1 & w_1 & w_2,\; w_3 \\
2 & w_3 & w_2,\; w_5 \\
3 & w_5 & w_2,\; w_6 \\
4 & w_6 & w_2 \\
5 & w_2 & w_4 \\
6 & w_4 & \varnothing \\
\end{array}
\]

The DFS traversal order is therefore:

\[
w_1 \;\to\; w_3 \;\to\; w_5 \;\to\; w_6 \;\to\; w_2 \;\to\; w_4
\]

DFS dives immediately from $w_1$ down the branch $w_3 \to w_5 \to w_6$
before backtracking to explore $w_2 \to w_4$. This depth-first guarantee
means DFS will always fully explore one branch before moving to the next,
in contrast to the level-by-level exploration of BFS.

\section{Simplification of CFG}

\subsection{Reduction of CFG}

A symbol $\psi \in (V \cup T)$ is useful if:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\psi$ is generating: $\psi \to^* w$ where $w \in T^*$
    \item $\psi$ is reachable: $S \to^* X \psi Y$ where $X, Y \in (V \cup T)^*$
\end{enumerate}

Algorithm:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Eliminate non-generating symbols
    \item Eliminate unreachable symbols
\end{enumerate}



\textbf{Example:}

Consider the context-free grammar with production set

\[
P = \{\, S \to XY \mid Z,\; X \to 1,\; Y \to 0 \mid ZY,\; E \to 1X \mid e \,\}.
\]

\begin{enumerate}

\item Step 1: Eliminate non-generating symbols

A nonterminal \(W\) is \emph{generating} if \(W \to^* w\) for some \(w \in T^*\).

\begin{enumerate}
    \item \(X\) is generating since \(X \to 1\).
    \item \(Y\) is generating since \(Y \to 0\).
    \item \(E\) is generating since \(E \to e\).
    \item \(S\) is generating since \(S \to XY \to 10\).
    \item \(Z\) is non-generating, as it has no productions.
\end{enumerate}

Thus, the set of generating nonterminals is \(\{S, X, Y, E\}\).
All productions containing the non-generating symbol \(Z\) are removed, yielding:

\[
P_1 = \{\, S \to XY,\; X \to 1,\; Y \to 0,\; E \to 1X \mid e \,\}.
\]

\item Step 2: Eliminate unreachable symbols

Starting from the start symbol \(S\):

\begin{enumerate}
    \item The reachable symbols are \(S, X, Y\).
    \item The symbol \(E\) is unreachable from \(S\).
\end{enumerate}

Removing all unreachable symbols and their productions gives:

\[
P_2 = \{\, S \to XY,\; X \to 1,\; Y \to 0 \,\}.
\]

\end{enumerate}

Hence, the reduced grammar is:

\[\boxed{G' = (\{S, X, Y\}, \{1, 0\}, S, \{S \to XY,\; X \to 1,\; Y \to 0\})}\]

\subsection{Removal Unit of Production}

A production $\psi \to \chi$ is a unit production if $\psi, \chi \in V$ (both are variables).

Algorithm:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Find all unit productions
    \item For each unit production $\psi \to \chi$, replace it with $\psi \to 1$ for all non-unit productions $\chi \to 1$
    \item Remove all unit productions
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}

Given

\[P = \{S \to XY, X \to 1, Y \to Z \mid 0, Z \to L, L \to T, T \to 1\}\]

\begin{enumerate}

\item Step 1: Identify unit productions

\[Y \to Z, \quad Z \to L, \quad L \to T\]

\item Step 2: Find unit closure for each variable

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Since $T \to 1$, we add $L \to 1$
    \item Since $L \to 1$, we add $Z \to 1$
    \item Since $Z \to 1$, we add $Y \to 1$
\end{enumerate}

Then \[P = \{S \to XY, X \to 1, Y \to 1 \mid 0, Z \to 1, L \to 1, T \to 1\}\]

Furthermore, we remove the unreachable symbols, that is $L$, $T$, and $Z$. 

\end{enumerate}

Therefore, the reduced grammar is: \[\boxed{G' = (\{S, X, Y\}, \{1, 0\}, S, \{S \to XY, X \to 1, Y \to 1 \mid 0\})}\]

\subsection{Removal of Null Productions}

A production $\psi \to \varepsilon$ is called a \emph{null production} (or $\varepsilon$-production).

Algorithm:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Identify all nullable variables (variables that can derive $\varepsilon$).
    \item For each production, generate all possible productions obtained by deleting
          nullable variables in every possible combination (excluding the empty right-hand
          side unless the start symbol is nullable).
    \item Remove all null productions.
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example}

Consider the grammar with production set

\[
P = \{\, S \to XYXZ,\; X \to 1X \mid \varepsilon,\; Y \to xY \mid \varepsilon,\; Z \to 0 \,\}.
\]

\begin{enumerate}

\item Step 1: Identify nullable variables

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $X \to \varepsilon$, hence $X$ is nullable.
    \item $Y \to \varepsilon$, hence $Y$ is nullable.
\end{enumerate}

Thus, the set of nullable variables is $\{X, Y\}$.

\item Step 2: Generate new productions

We consider all subsets of nullable variables $\{X,Y\}$ in each production.


From $S \to XYXZ$, the possible right-hand sides are:

\[
\begin{aligned}
S \to {} &XYXZ \mid YXZ \mid XXZ \mid XZ \mid YZ \mid Z
\end{aligned}
\]



From $X \to 1X$, removing the nullable variable $X$ yields:

\[
X \to 1X \mid 1
\]


From $Y \to xY$, removing the nullable variable $Y$ yields:

\[
Y \to xY \mid x
\]

\item Step 3: Remove all null productions

After removing the productions $X \to \varepsilon$ and $Y \to \varepsilon$, the resulting
grammar has production set:

\[
\fbox{$
\begin{aligned}
P' = \{\, 
&S \to XYXZ \mid YXZ \mid XXZ \mid XZ \mid YZ \mid Z, \\
&X \to 1X \mid 1,\;
Y \to xY \mid x,\;
Z \to 0
\,\}
\end{aligned}
$}
\]

\end{enumerate}

\section{Chomsky Normal Form (CNF)}

A CFG is in CNF if every production rule has one of these forms:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\psi \to \phi \gamma$, where $\psi, \phi, \gamma \in V$ (non-terminals)
    \item $\psi \to 1$, where $\psi \in V$ and $1 \in \Sigma$ (terminal)
    \item $S \to \varepsilon$, where $S$ is the start symbol and $S$ does not appear on the right-hand side of any production
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}

Consider the following grammar in CNF:

$$G = (V, \Sigma, R, S)$$

where:

\[V = \{f, g, h, j\}, \quad  \Sigma = \{1, 0\} \]

Production Rules:

\begin{align*}
    f &\to g h,  &\text{(Rule 1: two non-terminals)} \\
    f &\to h j,  &\text{(Rule 1: two non-terminals)} \\
    g &\to 1,  &\text{(Rule 2: single terminal)} \\
    h &\to 0, &\text{(Rule 2: single terminal)} \\
    j &\to f g, &\text{(Rule 1: two non-terminals)} \\
    j &\to 1, &\text{(Rule 2: single terminal)}
\end{align*}


\subsection{CFG to CNF}

There are 5 steps involved in the conversion of CFG to Chomsky Normal Form:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item If the start symbol $\psi$ occurs on the right-hand side of any production rule, create a new start symbol $\psi'$ and add a new production rule $\psi' \to \psi$.
    
    \item Remove null productions.
    
    \item Remove unit productions.
    
    \item Replace each production $\chi \to \eta_1 \eta_2 \ldots \eta_n$ where $n > 2$ with:
    
    \begin{align*}
        \chi &\to \eta_1 L_1 \\
        L_1 &\to \eta_2 L_2 \\
        L_2 &\to \eta_3 L_3 \\
        &\vdots \\
        L_{n-2} &\to \eta_{n-1} \eta_n
    \end{align*}
    
    where $L_1, L_2, \ldots, L_{n-2}$ are new non-terminals.
    
    \item Replace each production $\chi \to x$ where $x$ contains terminals mixed with non-terminals with:
    
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item For each terminal $a$ in $x$, create a new non-terminal $T_a$ and production $T_a \to a$
        \item Replace $a$ with $T_a$ in the original production
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example: }

Given 

\[
P = \{f \to \chi f \chi \mid 1g,\; \chi \to g \mid f,\; g \to 0 \mid \varepsilon \}
\]

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Step 1: Since $f$ is the start symbol and appears in RHS of $f \to \chi f \chi$, we add a new start symbol $f'$ and add $f' \to f$
    
    Hence,

    \[
    P = \{f' \to f,\; f \to \chi f \chi \mid 1g,\; \chi \to g \mid f,\; g \to 0 \mid \varepsilon \}
    \]

    \item Step 2: Remove the null productions

    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $g$ is nullable (has production $g \to \varepsilon$)
        \item $\chi$ is nullable (has production $\chi \to g$, and $g$ is nullable)
        
        
        \item After removing $g \to \varepsilon$: for every production 
        containing $g$, add a copy with $g$ removed:

        \[
        f' \to f,\;
        f \to \chi f \chi \mid 1g \mid 1,\;
        \chi \to g \mid f \mid \varepsilon,\;
        g \to 0
        \]

        \item After removing $\chi \to \varepsilon$: for every production 
        containing $\chi$, add a copy with $\chi$ removed:

        \[
        f' \to f,\;
        f \to \chi f \chi \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid f \mid 1g \mid 1,\;
        \chi \to g \mid f,\;
        g \to 0
        \]

    \end{enumerate}

    \item Step 3: Remove unit productions: $f' \to f$, $f \to f$, $\chi \to g$, and $\chi \to f$
        
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item For $f' \to f$: Replace with all non-unit productions of $f$:
        \[
        f' \to \chi f\chi \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1
        \]

        \item For $f \to f$: Remove it (self-loop unit production)

        \item For $\chi \to g$: Replace with all non-unit productions of $g$:
        \[
        \chi \to 0
        \]

        \item For $\chi \to f$: Replace with all non-unit productions of $f$:
        \[
        \chi \to \chi f\chi \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1
        \]
    \end{enumerate}
    
    After removing all unit productions:

    \[
    \begin{aligned}
        f' &\to \chi f\chi \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1 \\
        f  &\to \chi f\chi \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1 \\
        \chi  &\to 0 \mid \chi f\chi \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1 \\
        g  &\to 0
    \end{aligned}
    \]

    \item Step 4: Replace productions with more than 2 symbols on RHS
    
    For $f' \to \chi f\chi$, $f \to \chi f\chi$, and $\chi \to \chi f\chi$: introduce $L_1 \to f\chi$, so $\chi f\chi$ becomes $\chi L_1$:
    
    \[
    \begin{aligned}
        f' &\to \chi L_1 \\
        f  &\to \chi L_1 \\
        \chi  &\to \chi L_1 \\
        L_1   &\to f\chi
    \end{aligned}
    \]
    
    After replacing all such productions:
    
    \[
    \begin{aligned}
        f' &\to \chi L_1 \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1 \\
        f  &\to \chi L_1 \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1 \\
        \chi  &\to 0 \mid \chi L_1 \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid 1g \mid 1 \\
        g  &\to 0 \\
        L_1   &\to f\chi
    \end{aligned}
    \]

    \item Step 5: Replace terminals mixed with non-terminals in productions
    
    The productions $f' \to 1g$, $f \to 1g$, and $\chi \to 1g$ contain terminal $1$ mixed 
    with non-terminal $g$. Introduce $T_1 \to 1$ and replace $1$ with $T_1$ in these productions.
    Note that standalone terminal productions such as $f' \to 1$, $\chi \to 0$, and $g \to 0$ 
    are already valid CNF and do not need to be changed.
    
    Therefore, the final CNF:

    \[
    \fbox{$
    \begin{aligned}
        f' &\to \chi L_1 \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid T_1 g \mid 1 \\
        f  &\to \chi L_1 \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid T_1 g \mid 1 \\
        \chi  &\to 0 \mid \chi L_1 \mid f\chi \mid \chi f \mid T_1 g \mid 1 \\
        g  &\to 0 \\
        L_1   &\to f\chi \\
        T_1   &\to 1
    \end{aligned}
    $}
    \]

\end{enumerate}

A CFG is in CNF if every production rule has one of these forms:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\psi \to \phi \gamma$, where $\psi, \phi, \gamma \in V$ (non-terminals)
    \item $\psi \to 1$, where $\psi \in V$ and $1 \in \Sigma$ (terminal)
    \item $S \to \varepsilon$, where $S$ is the start symbol and $S$ does not appear on the right-hand side of any production
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:}

Consider the following grammar in CNF:

$$G = (V, \Sigma, R, S)$$

where:

\[V = \{f, g, h, j\}, \quad  \Sigma = \{1, 0\} \]

Production Rules:

\begin{align*}
    f &\to g h,  &\text{(Rule 1: two non-terminals)} \\
    f &\to h j,  &\text{(Rule 1: two non-terminals)} \\
    g &\to 1,  &\text{(Rule 2: single terminal)} \\
    h &\to 0, &\text{(Rule 2: single terminal)} \\
    j &\to f g, &\text{(Rule 1: two non-terminals)} \\
    j &\to 1, &\text{(Rule 2: single terminal)}
\end{align*}

\section{Greibach Normal Form (GNF)}

A CFG is in GNF if the productions are in the following forms:

\begin{align*}
\psi &\to 1 \\
\psi &\to 1\phi_1\phi_2 \ldots \phi_n
\end{align*}
where:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\psi, \phi_1, \phi_2, \ldots, \phi_n$ are non-terminal symbols
    \item $1$ is a terminal symbol
    \item $n \geq 1$ (one or more non-terminals may follow the terminal)
\end{enumerate}

Additionally, the start symbol $S$ may have the production $S \to \varepsilon$ if $\varepsilon$ is in the language.

\textbf{In General:}

Consider the following CFG:
\begin{align*}
f &\to gh \mid 1 \\
g &\to 1g \mid 0 \\
h &\to 0
\end{align*}

This grammar is not in GNF because:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $f \to gh$ starts with a non-terminal (not a terminal)
    \item $h \to 0$ is acceptable (starts with terminal $0$)
\end{enumerate}

The same language can be represented in GNF as:

\begin{align*}
f &\to 1gh \mid 1h \mid 0h \mid 1 \\
g &\to 1g \mid 1 \mid 0 \\
h &\to 0
\end{align*}

\subsection{CFG to GNF}

There are 3 major steps involved in the conversion of CFG to Greibach Normal Form:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Remove Unit and Null Productions.
    
    \item Convert to Chomsky Normal Form (CNF). 
    
    \item Convert CNF to GNF:
    
    \begin{enumerate}[label=\alph*)]
        \item Order the productions:
        
        Alter the rules so that the non-terminals are in ascending order. For any production of the form:
        
        \[
        \psi_i \to \psi_jT
        \]

        where $T \in (V \cup \Sigma)^*$, we must ensure that:
        
        \[
        i < j
        \]
        
        If $i \geq j$: Replace $\psi_j$ with its production rules to eliminate the violation.
        
        
        If $\psi_j \to L_1 \mid L_2 \mid \cdots \mid L_k$, then substitute:
        
        \[
        \psi_i \to L_1T \mid L_2T \mid \cdots \mid L_kT
        \]
        
        \item Remove left recursion:
        
        If after substitution, we have direct left recursion of the form:

        \[
        \psi_i \to \psi_iT_1 \mid \psi_iT_2 \mid \cdots \mid \psi_iT_r \mid L_1 \mid L_2 \mid \cdots \mid L_s
        \]
        
        where $L_j$ does not start with $\psi_i$, then eliminate it using:
        
        \[
        \begin{aligned}
        \psi_i &\to L_1 \mid L_2 \mid \cdots \mid L_s \mid L_1\psi_{n+1} \mid L_2\psi_{n+1} \mid \cdots \mid L_s\psi_{n+1} \\
        \psi_{n+1} &\to T_1 \mid T_2 \mid \cdots \mid T_r \mid T_1\psi_{n+1} \mid T_2\psi_{n+1} \mid \cdots \mid T_r\psi_{n+1}
        \end{aligned}
        \]
        
        where $\psi_{n+1}$ is a new non-terminal.
        
        \item Convert to terminal-leading form:
        
        For each production $\psi_i \to \psi_jT$ where $i < j$, substitute $\psi_j$ with its productions until all productions are of the form:
        
        \[
        \psi_i \to 1\gamma
        \]

        where $1 \in \Sigma$ (terminal) and $\gamma \in V^*$ (string of non-terminals).
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example:} 

Given

\begin{align*}
S &\to CA \mid BB \\
B &\to 0 \mid SB \\
C &\to 0\\
A &\to 1
\end{align*}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Step 1: Rename non-terminals to $\chi_i$ form
    
    We change the non-terminals into a numbered sequence: $S \to \chi_1$, $C \to \chi_2$, $A \to \chi_3$, $B \to \chi_4$

    \begin{align*}
    \chi_1 &\to \chi_2\chi_3 \mid \chi_4\chi_4 \\
    \chi_2 &\to 0 \\
    \chi_3 &\to 1\\
    \chi_4 &\to 0 \mid \chi_1\chi_4 
    \end{align*}

    \item Step 2: Check ordering rule ($i < j$)
    
    Analyze each production $\chi_i \to \chi_j\alpha$:

    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\chi_1 \to \chi_2\chi_3$: $1 < 2$ 
        \item $\chi_1 \to \chi_4\chi_4$: $1 < 4$ 
        \item $\chi_2 \to 0$: terminal 
        \item $\chi_3 \to 1$: terminal 
        \item $\chi_4 \to 0$: terminal 
        \item $\chi_4 \to \chi_1\chi_4$: $4 \not< 1$  (violates rule)
    \end{enumerate}
    
    The production $\chi_4 \to \chi_1\chi_4$ violates the ordering rule because $i = 4 \geq j = 1$.

    \item Step 3: Substitute $\chi_1$ into $\chi_4$ to fix violation
    
    Since $\chi_1 \to \chi_2\chi_3 \mid \chi_4\chi_4$, substitute into $\chi_4 \to \chi_1\chi_4$:

    \begin{align*}
    \chi_4 &\to 0 \mid \chi_2\chi_3\chi_4 \mid \chi_4\chi_4\chi_4
    \end{align*}

    \item Step 4: Remove left recursion from $\chi_4$
    
    The production $\chi_4 \to \chi_4\chi_4\chi_4$ has direct left recursion, introduce new variable $\chi_5 = \psi_{n+1}$:

    \begin{align*}
    \chi_4 &\to 0 \mid \chi_2\chi_3\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5 \mid \chi_2\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5 \\
    \chi_5 &\to \chi_4\chi_4 \mid \chi_4\chi_4\chi_5
    \end{align*}

    Current grammar:
    \begin{align*}
    \chi_1 &\to \chi_2\chi_3 \mid \chi_4\chi_4 \\
    \chi_2 &\to 0 \\
    \chi_3 &\to 1 \\
    \chi_4 &\to 0 \mid \chi_2\chi_3\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5 \mid \chi_2\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5 \\
    \chi_5 &\to \chi_4\chi_4 \mid \chi_4\chi_4\chi_5
    \end{align*}

    \item Step 5: Convert to terminal-leading form
    
    For each production $\psi_i \to \psi_jT$ where $i < j$, substitute $\psi_j$ with its productions.
    
    \begin{enumerate}

    \item Substitute $\chi_2 \to 0$ wherever $\chi_2$ appears:
    
    \begin{align*}
    \chi_1 &\to 0\chi_3 \mid \chi_4\chi_4 \\
    \chi_4 &\to 0 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5
    \end{align*}
    

    Since in $\chi_1 \to \chi_4\chi_4$, $\chi_4$ appears twice, we need to substitute $\chi_4$ again. 

    \item Substitute $\chi_4$ into productions starting with $\chi_4$:
    
    \begin{align*}
    \chi_1 &\to 0\chi_3 \mid 0\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4
    \end{align*}

    \item Substitute $\chi_4$ into $\chi_5$ productions:
    
    For $\chi_5 \to \chi_4\chi_4$, substitute first $\chi_4$:

    \begin{align*}
    \chi_5 &\to 0\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4
    \end{align*}
    
    For $\chi_5 \to \chi_4\chi_4\chi_5$, substitute first $\chi_4$:

    \begin{align*}
    \chi_5 &\to 0\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4\chi_5
    \end{align*}

    Combining both cases:

    \begin{align*}
    \chi_5 &\to 0\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4 \\
        &\quad \mid 0\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4\chi_5
    \end{align*}

    \end{enumerate}

    \item Step 6: All productions are now of the form $\psi_i \to 1\gamma$ where $1 \in \Sigma$ (terminal $0$ or $1$) and $\gamma \in V^*$ (string of non-terminals):
    
    \[
    \fbox{$
    \begin{aligned}
    \chi_1 &\to 0\chi_3 \mid 0\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4 \\
    \chi_2 &\to 0 \\
    \chi_3 &\to 1 \\
    \chi_4 &\to 0 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5 \\
    \chi_5 &\to 0\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4 \\
        &\quad \mid 0\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_5\chi_4\chi_5 \mid 0\chi_3\chi_4\chi_5\chi_4\chi_5
    \end{aligned}
    $}
    \]
    
\end{enumerate}

\section{Pushdown Automata (PDA)}

A Pushdown Automaton (PDA) is formally defined as a 7-tuple:

\[
M = (W, \Sigma, \Gamma, V, S, Z_0, F)
\]

where:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $W$ is a finite set of states
    \item $\Sigma$ is a finite input alphabet
    \item $\Gamma$ is a finite stack alphabet
    \item $V: W \times (\Sigma \cup \{\varepsilon\}) \times \Gamma \to \mathcal{P}(W \times \Gamma^*)$ is the transition function
    \item $S \in W$ is the initial state
    \item $Z_0 \in \Gamma$ is the initial stack symbol
    \item $F \subseteq W$ is the set of accepting states
\end{enumerate}

The transition function $V(w, x, X)$ returns a set of pairs $(w', \gamma)$ where:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $w$ is the current state
    \item $x \in \Sigma \cup \{\varepsilon\}$ is the input symbol (or $\varepsilon$ for no input)
    \item $X \in \Gamma$ is the top symbol of the stack
    \item $w'$ is the next state
    \item $\gamma \in \Gamma^*$ is the string to replace $X$ on the stack
\end{enumerate}
