\[\langle M, x \rangle \in X \leftrightarrow f(\langle M, x \rangle) \in Y\]

Given $\langle M, x \rangle$, construct $M'$ where:

\[M'(y) = M(x) \quad \text{for all inputs } y\]

Then, 

\[\langle M, x \rangle \in X \leftrightarrow M \text{ halts on } x \leftrightarrow M' \text{ halts on } \varepsilon \leftrightarrow \langle M' \rangle \in Y\]

Therefore, \[X \leq_m Y\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[
    node distance=2cm,
    box/.style={rectangle, draw, circle, minimum width=2cm, minimum height=1cm, align=center},
    arrow/.style={->, >=stealth}
]

\node[box] (input) {$\langle M, x \rangle$};
\node[box, right=3cm of input] (mprime) {$\langle M' \rangle$};
\node[box, below=1.5cm of input] (X) {$X$};
\node[box, below=1.5cm of mprime] (Y) {$Y$};

\draw[arrow] (input) -- node[above] {$f$} node[below] {\small construct $M'$} (mprime);
\draw[arrow] (input) -- (X);
\draw[arrow] (mprime) -- (Y);
\draw[arrow, dashed] (X) -- node[above] {$\leq_m$} (Y);

\node[below=0.3cm of X, align=center] {\small $M$ halts on $x$};
\node[below=0.3cm of Y, align=center] {\small $M'$ halts on $\varepsilon$};
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\emph{Theorem.} Let $X \leq_m Y$. Then the following hold:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $X \text{ decidable} \not\to Y \text{ decidable}$
    \item $X \text{ undecidable} \to Y \text{ undecidable}$
    \item $Y \text{ decidable} \to X \text{ decidable}$
    \item $Y \text{ undecidable} \not\to X \text{ undecidable}$
\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example 2:}

\begin{align*}
A_{TM} &= \{\langle M, x \rangle \mid M \text{ is a TM and } M \text{ accepts } x\} \\
HALT_{TM} &= \{\langle M, x \rangle \mid M \text{ is a TM and } M \text{ halts on } x\}
\end{align*}

We show $A_{TM} \leq_m HALT_{TM}$. We construct a computable function $f$ such that:

\[
\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM} \leftrightarrow f(\langle M, x\rangle) \in HALT_{TM}
\]

\noindent Given an instance $\langle M, x\rangle$ of $A_{TM}$, we construct a new Turing machine $M'$ as follows:

\bigskip

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.6\textwidth}
$M'$ = on input $x$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Run $M$ on $x$
    \item If $M$ accepts, accept
    \item If $M$ rejects, loop forever
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

The reduction maps $\langle M, x\rangle \to \langle M', x \rangle$. We verify the correctness:

($\to$) Suppose $\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM}$, i.e., $M$ accepts $x$. Then $M'$ simulates $M$ on $x$, reaches the accept state, and halts. So $\langle M', x \rangle \in HALT_{TM}$.

($\leftarrow$) Suppose $\langle M', x \rangle \in HALT_{TM}$, i.e., $M'$ halts on $x$. By construction, $M'$ only halts if $M$ accepts $x$ (otherwise it loops). So $M$ accepts $x$, meaning $\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM}$.

Therefore:

\[
\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM} \leftrightarrow \langle M', x \rangle \in HALT_{TM}
\]

Since $f : \langle M, x\rangle \to \langle M', x \rangle$ is computable, we conclude $A_{TM} \leq_m HALT_{TM}$.

\bigskip

\textbf{Example 3:}

\begin{align*}
A_{TM} &= \{\langle M, x \rangle \mid M \text{ is a TM and } M \text{ accepts } x\} \\
\overline{A_{TM}} &= \{\langle M, x \rangle \mid M \text{ is a TM and } M \text{ does not accept } x\}
\end{align*}

We show $A_{TM} \leq_m \overline{A_{TM}}$. We construct a computable function $f$ such that:

\[
\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM} \leftrightarrow f(\langle M, x\rangle) \in \overline{A_{TM}}
\]

\noindent Given an instance $\langle M, x\rangle$ of $A_{TM}$, we construct a new Turing machine $M'$ as follows:

\bigskip

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.6\textwidth}
$M'$ = on input $x$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Run $M$ on $x$
    \item If $M$ accepts, reject
    \item If $M$ rejects, accept
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

The reduction maps $\langle M, x\rangle \to \langle M', x \rangle$. We verify the correctness:

($\to$) Suppose $\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM}$, i.e., $M$ accepts $x$. Then $M'$ simulates $M$ on $x$ and rejects. So $\langle M', x \rangle \notin A_{TM}$, meaning $\langle M', x \rangle \in \overline{A_{TM}}$.

($\leftarrow$) Suppose $\langle M', x \rangle \in \overline{A_{TM}}$, i.e., $M'$ does not accept $x$. By construction, $M'$ rejects whenever $M$ accepts. So $M$ accepts $x$, meaning $\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM}$.

Therefore:

\[
\langle M, x\rangle \in A_{TM} \leftrightarrow \langle M', x \rangle \in \overline{A_{TM}}
\]

Since $f : \langle M, x\rangle \to \langle M', x \rangle$ is computable, we conclude $A_{TM} \leq_m \overline{A_{TM}}$.

\bigskip

\textbf{Example 4:}

\begin{align*}
HALT_{TM} &= \{\langle M, x \rangle \mid M \text{ is a TM and } M \text{ halts on } x\} \\
E_{TM} &= \{\langle M \rangle \mid M \text{ is a TM and } L(M) = \varnothing\}
\end{align*}

We show $HALT_{TM} \leq_m E_{TM}$. We construct a computable function $f$ such that:

\[
\langle M, x\rangle \in HALT_{TM} \leftrightarrow f(\langle M, x\rangle) \in E_{TM}
\]

\noindent Given an instance $\langle M, x\rangle$ of $HALT_{TM}$, we construct a new Turing machine $M'$ as follows:

\bigskip

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.6\textwidth}
$M'$ = on input $y$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Run $M$ on $x$ for $|y|$ steps
    \item If $M$ halts, accept
    \item Otherwise, reject
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

The reduction maps $\langle M, x\rangle \to \langle M' \rangle$. We verify the correctness:

($\to$) Suppose $\langle M, x\rangle \in HALT_{TM}$, i.e., $M$ halts on $x$ in $k$ steps. Then for any $y$ with $|y| \geq k$, $M'$ accepts. So $L(M') \neq \varnothing$, meaning $\langle M' \rangle \notin E_{TM}$.

($\leftarrow$) Suppose $\langle M' \rangle \notin E_{TM}$, i.e., $L(M') \neq \varnothing$. Then there exists some $y$ such that $M'$ accepts $y$, meaning $M$ halted on $x$ within $|y|$ steps. So $\langle M, x\rangle \in HALT_{TM}$.

Therefore:

\[
\langle M, x\rangle \in HALT_{TM} \leftrightarrow \langle M' \rangle \notin E_{TM}
\]

Since $f : \langle M, x\rangle \to \langle M' \rangle$ is computable, we conclude $HALT_{TM} \leq_m E_{TM}$.

\section{Post Correspondence Problem (PCP)}

The Post Correspondence Problem (PCP) is a decision problem defined as follows.

A PCP instance consists of a finite set of dominoes

\[
P = \left\{ \bigg[\frac{x_1}{y_1}\bigg], \bigg[\frac{x_2}{y_2}\bigg], \ldots, \bigg[\frac{x_n}{y_n}\bigg] \right\},
\]

where each $x_i, y_i \in \Sigma^*$ for some finite alphabet $\Sigma$. The problem asks whether there exists a nonempty sequence of indices

\[
i_1, i_2, \ldots, i_k \quad (k \geq 1)
\]

such that

\[
x_{i_1} x_{i_2} \cdots x_{i_k}
=
y_{i_1} y_{i_2} \cdots y_{i_k}.
\]


\textbf{Example 1}

Consider the following set of dominoes:
\[
P = \left\{
\bigg[\frac{1}{01}\bigg],
\bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg],
\bigg[\frac{0}{0}\bigg]
\right\}.
\]

We want to determine whether this PCP instance has a solution. One possible sequence of indices is $[2, 1, 3]$:

\[
\bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{1}{01}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{0}{0}\bigg]
\;=\;
\frac{10 \cdot 1 \cdot 0}{1 \cdot 01 \cdot 0}
\;=\;
\frac{1010}{1010}
\]

Since both concatenations are equal, this sequence is a solution, and the answer to this PCP instance is \emph{yes}.


\textbf{Example 2}

Consider the following PCP instance:

\[
P = \left\{
\bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg],
\bigg[\frac{011}{10}\bigg],
\bigg[\frac{01}{011}\bigg]
\right\}.
\]

We test several sequences of indices.

\begin{enumerate}
\item Sequence $[1,2,3]$:

\[
\bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{011}{10}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{01}{011}\bigg]
\;=\;
\frac{10 \cdot 011 \cdot 01}{1 \cdot 10 \cdot 011}
\;=\;
\frac{1001101}{110011}
\]
These strings are not equal.

\item Sequence $[2,1,3]$:

\[
\bigg[\frac{011}{10}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{01}{011}\bigg]
\;=\;
\frac{011 \cdot 10 \cdot 01}{10 \cdot 1 \cdot 011}
\;=\;
\frac{0111001}{101011}
\]
These strings are not equal.

\item Sequence $[3,3,1,2]$:

\[
\bigg[\frac{01}{011}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{01}{011}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg]
\cdot
\bigg[\frac{011}{10}\bigg]
\;=\;
\frac{01 \cdot 01 \cdot 10 \cdot 011}{011 \cdot 011 \cdot 1 \cdot 10}
\;=\;
\frac{01011011}{01101110}
\]

These strings are not equal.
\end{enumerate}

Hence, no solution has been found for this PCP instance.

\textbf{Example 3}

Consider the PCP instance

\[
P = \left\{
\bigg[\frac{0}{00}\bigg],
\bigg[\frac{1}{11}\bigg]
\right\}.
\]

For any sequence of indices $[i_1, i_2, \ldots, i_k]$, suppose the sequence contains
$n$ occurrences of index $1$ and $m$ occurrences of index $2$.

Then

\begin{align*}
x_{i_1} x_{i_2} \cdots x_{i_k} &= \text{a string of length } n + m, \\
y_{i_1} y_{i_2} \cdots y_{i_k} &= \text{a string of length } 2(n + m).
\end{align*}

Since the lengths of the two strings are different, no solution exists for this PCP instance.



\subsection{Modified Post Correspondence Problem (MPCP)}

The \emph{Modified Post Correspondence Problem} (MPCP) is a constrained variant of PCP in which
the first domino of any solution is fixed. Formally, given a finite list of domino pairs

\[
P = \left\{ \bigg[\frac{u_1}{v_1}\bigg],\, \bigg[\frac{u_2}{v_2}\bigg],\, \dots,\, \bigg[\frac{u_n}{v_n}\bigg] \right\},
\quad u_i, v_i \in \Sigma^*,
\]

an \emph{MPCP solution} is a sequence of indices $i_2, i_3, \dots, i_k \in \{1, \dots, n\}$ such that

\[
u_1\, u_{i_2}\, u_{i_3} \cdots u_{i_k} \;=\; v_1\, v_{i_2}\, v_{i_3} \cdots v_{i_k}.
\]

That is, the first domino $(u_1, v_1)$ is \emph{required} as the starting piece; the remaining
indices $i_2, \dots, i_k$ may be chosen freely from $\{1, \dots, n\}$ with repetition.
This is the only difference from ordinary PCP, where all indices may be chosen freely.


\textbf{Example 1:}

Consider the following MPCP instance over $\Sigma = \{0, 1\}$:

\[
  \bigg[\frac{u_1}{v_1}\bigg] = \bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg],
  \qquad
  \bigg[\frac{u_2}{v_2}\bigg] = \bigg[\frac{1}{11}\bigg],
  \qquad
  \bigg[\frac{u_3}{v_3}\bigg] = \bigg[\frac{01}{00}\bigg].
\]

Since domino 1 is required to appear first, we try the sequence $1, 3, 2$:

\[
  \bigg[\frac{10}{1}\bigg]
  \cdot
  \bigg[\frac{01}{00}\bigg]
  \cdot
  \bigg[\frac{1}{11}\bigg]
  \;=\;
  \frac{10 \cdot 01 \cdot 1}{1 \cdot 00 \cdot 11}
  \;=\;
  \frac{10011}{10011}
\]

The top and bottom concatenations are equal, so the sequence $1, 3, 2$ is a valid MPCP solution.
Note that domino 1 appears first, as required by MPCP.


\textbf{Example 2:}

Let

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  node distance=3.5cm,
  every state/.style={circle, draw, minimum size=1cm},
  auto
]
  \node[state, initial] (q0) {$w_0$};
  \node[state] (q1) [right of=q0] {$w_1$};
  \node[state, accepting] (q2) [right of=q1] {$w_2$};

  \path[->]
    (q0) edge node {$0 \to x,\, R$} (q1)
    (q1) edge node {$1 \to x,\, L$} (q2);
\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

where $\Gamma = \{0, 1, x, \triangle\}$ and the input string is $w = 010$.

\begin{enumerate}

    \item Step 1: Start domino.

    We place the initial domino. The top starts with just $\#$, while the bottom encodes the
    full start configuration $S_1 = \#\;w_0\;0\;1\;0\;\#$. The bottom is always one
    configuration ahead of the top; this is the invariant that drives the whole construction.

    \[
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#\;w_0\;0\;1\;0\;\#}\right]
    \]

    \item Step 2: Transition $V(w_0, 0) = (w_1, x, R)$.

    The machine reads $0$ under state $w_0$, writes $x$, and moves right to state $w_1$,
    producing configuration $S_2 = \#\;x\;w_1\;1\;0\;\#$. We apply the right-move transition
    domino, which appends $w_0\;0$ to the top and $x\;w_1$ to the bottom, encoding this
    transition. The copying dominoes then copy the remaining symbols $1$, $0$, $\#$ so the top
    catches up through the end of $S_1$ while the bottom finishes building $S_2$.

    \[
    \left[\frac{w_0\;0}{x\;w_1}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{1}{1}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{0}{0}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    \item Step 3: Transition $V(w_1, 1) = (w_2, x, L)$.

    The machine reads $1$ under state $w_1$, writes $x$, and moves left to state $w_2$,
    producing the accepting configuration $S_3 = \#\;w_2\;x\;x\;0\;\#$. Since the head moves
    left, the domino must also carry the symbol $y$ immediately to the left of the head; it
    cannot be ignored because it determines where $w_2$ lands. We therefore need one domino
    for every possible $y \in \Gamma$. In our run $y = x$, since cell 1 was written $x$ in
    Step 2. Since $\Gamma = \{0, 1, x, \triangle\}$, the four left-move dominoes are:

    \[
    \left[\frac{0\;w_1\;1}{w_2\;0\;x}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{1\;w_1\;1}{w_2\;1\;x}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{x\;w_1\;1}{w_2\;x\;x}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{\triangle\;w_1\;1}{w_2\;\triangle\;x}\right]
    \]

    The active one in this run is $\left[\frac{x\;w_1\;1}{w_2\;x\;x}\right]$.
    The copying dominoes then copy $0$ and $\#$ to complete $S_3$.

    \[
    \left[\frac{0}{0}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    \item Step 4: Copying dominoes.

    Any symbol not involved in a transition must be copied unchanged from $S_i$ to $S_{i+1}$.
    These dominoes propagate untouched tape cells, keeping the configuration boundaries aligned.

    \[
    \left[\frac{0}{0}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{1}{1}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{x}{x}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{\triangle}{\triangle}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    \item Step 5: Acceptance dominoes.

    Once $w_2$ appears in $S_3$, the machine has accepted. We now erase all remaining tape
    symbols so that the top and bottom strings can become equal. The acceptance dominoes consume
    one symbol at a time from either side of $w_2$, replacing the pair with just $w_2$,
    effectively shrinking the configuration until only the separators remain.

    For symbols to the right of $w_2$:
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{w_2\;0}{w_2}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{w_2\;1}{w_2}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{w_2\;x}{w_2}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{w_2\;\triangle}{w_2}\right]
    \]

    For symbols to the left of $w_2$:
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{0\;w_2}{w_2}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{1\;w_2}{w_2}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{x\;w_2}{w_2}\right]
    \qquad
    \left[\frac{\triangle\;w_2}{w_2}\right]
    \]

    Once all tape symbols have been erased, only $\#\;w_2\;\#$ remains. The termination
    domino collapses this to a single $\#$, making the top and bottom strings equal and
    completing the solution:
    \[
    \left[\frac{\#\;w_2\;\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    \item Step 6: Complete solution.

    Putting it all together, the full sequence of dominoes is as follows.

    Round 1: top catches up through $S_1$, bottom builds $S_2$.
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#\;w_0\;0\;1\;0\;\#}\right]
    \left[\frac{w_0\;0}{x\;w_1}\right]
    \left[\frac{1}{1}\right]
    \left[\frac{0}{0}\right]
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    Round 2: top catches up through $S_2$, bottom builds $S_3$.
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#\;x\;w_1\;1\;0\;\#}\right]
    \left[\frac{x\;w_1\;1}{w_2\;x\;x}\right]
    \left[\frac{0}{0}\right]
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

   Round 3: erase first $x$ from $S_3$.
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#\;w_2\;x\;x\;0\;\#}\right]
    \left[\frac{w_2\;x}{w_2}\right]
    \left[\frac{x}{x}\right]
    \left[\frac{0}{0}\right]
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    Round 4: erase second $x$.
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#\;w_2\;x\;0\;\#}\right]
    \left[\frac{w_2\;x}{w_2}\right]
    \left[\frac{0}{0}\right]
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    Round 5: erase $0$.
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#\;w_2\;0\;\#}\right]
    \left[\frac{w_2\;0}{w_2}\right]
    \left[\frac{\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

    Round 6: termination.
    
    \[
    \left[\frac{\#\;w_2\;\#}{\#}\right]
    \]

\end{enumerate}

All tape symbols have been erased. The termination domino fires, making the accumulated
top and bottom strings identical. The MPCP solution is complete, witnessing that $M$
accepts input $010$.

\section{Linear Bounded Automaton (LBA)}

A \emph{Linear Bounded Automaton} (LBA) is a restricted model of computation based on the
Turing machine, in which the tape head is confined to the portion of the tape occupied by the
input. Unlike a full Turing machine, which may use an unbounded amount of tape, an LBA may
only read and write on the cells that initially contain the input string; it cannot move beyond
the left or right endmarkers that delimit the input.

The tape head begins at the leftmost cell of the input. The machine operates like a Turing
machine, except that the transition function must never move the head left of the left endmarker
$\triangle$ or right of the right endmarker $\triangle$. Formally, if the head is scanning
$\triangle$ it may not move left, and if it is scanning $\triangle$ it may not move right.
The tape is therefore bounded by $|\,x\,|$ cells, where $x$ is the input string.


Because the tape of an LBA is bounded by the length of the input, the total number of
distinct configurations is finite. A configuration is a triple $(w, \alpha, i)$ where
$w \in W$ is the current state, $\alpha \in \Gamma^{|\,x\,|}$ is the tape contents, and
$i$ is the head position. The number of configurations is at most

\[
|W| \times |\Gamma|^{|\,x\,|} \times |\,x\,|,
\]

which is finite for any fixed input $x$. This finiteness has direct decidability consequences.


\textbf{Example:}

Let

\[
L = \{a^n b^n c^n \mid n \geq 1\}
\]

\noindent\textbf{Procedure:}

\begin{enumerate}

  \item Scan left to right. If all symbols are marked ($\dot{a}$, $\dot{b}$, $\dot{c}$),
    accept.

  \item Find the leftmost unmarked $a$. If none exists but unmarked $b$ or $c$ remain,
    reject.

  \item Mark that $a$ with $\dot{a}$. Continue scanning right to find the leftmost
    unmarked $b$.

  \item If no unmarked $b$ exists, reject. Otherwise mark it with $\dot{b}$.
    Continue scanning right to find the leftmost unmarked $c$.

  \item If no unmarked $c$ exists, reject. Otherwise mark it with $\dot{c}$.

  \item Return the head to the left endmarker $\triangle$ and repeat from Step 1.

\end{enumerate}


\noindent\textbf{Trace on input $aabbcc$:}

Initial tape:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (l) {\small $\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=l] (a1) {\small $a$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $a$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (b1) {\small $b$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b1] (b2) {\small $b$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b2] (c1) {\small $c$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {\small $c$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (r) {\small $\triangle$};
    % head
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of a1] (M) {\small $M$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (a1.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Round 1: mark the first $a$, $b$, $c$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (l) {\small $\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=l] (a1) {\small $\dot{a}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $a$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (b1) {\small $\dot{b}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b1] (b2) {\small $b$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b2] (c1) {\small $\dot{c}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {\small $c$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (r) {\small $\triangle$};
    % head
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of a2] (M) {\small $M$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (a2.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Round 2: mark the second $a$, $b$, $c$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (l) {\small $\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=l] (a1) {\small $\dot{a}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $\dot{a}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (b1) {\small $\dot{b}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b1] (b2) {\small $\dot{b}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b2] (c1) {\small $\dot{c}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {\small $\dot{c}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (r) {\small $\triangle$};
    % head
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of r] (M) {\small $M$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (r.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Round 3: all symbols are marked. The machine accepts.

\section{Recursion Theorem}

Formally, the Recursion Theorem states that for any Turing machine $M$, 
there exists a Turing machine $R$ such that for every input $x$,
\[R(x) = M(\langle R \rangle, x)\]

Intuitively, $R$ can obtain its own description $\langle R \rangle$ and pass it to $M$ as part of the input.


\textbf{Example 1:} 

We construct a Turing machine \textsc{Self} that prints its own description $\langle \textsc{Self} \rangle$. 

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.72\textwidth}
$\textsc{Self}$ = on input $x$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Obtain own description $\langle \textsc{Self} \rangle = \langle AB \rangle$
          using parts $A$ and $B$:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item[$A$:] Write $\langle B \rangle$ onto the tape.
        \item[$B$:] Read $\langle B \rangle$ from the tape; compute $f(\langle B \rangle) = \langle A \rangle$.
    \end{enumerate}
    \item Combine $\langle A \rangle$ and $\langle B \rangle$ to form
          $\langle AB \rangle = \langle \textsc{Self} \rangle$.
    \item Write $\langle \textsc{Self} \rangle$ on the tape and halt.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}


\begin{enumerate}

\item Step 1: Part $A$ runs. The tape is blank; $A$ writes $\langle B \rangle$ onto the tape.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (l)  {\small $\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=l]  (b1) {\small $\langle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b1] (b2) {\small $B$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b2] (b3) {\small $\rangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b3] (e1) {\small $\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=e1] (e2) {\small $\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=e2] (r)  {\small $\triangle$};
    
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of b3] (M) {\small $A$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (b3.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

$A$ finishes; tape holds $\langle B \rangle$. Control passes to $B$.

\item Step 2: Part $B$ reads $\langle B \rangle$ from the tape and computes $f(\langle B \rangle) = \langle A \rangle$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (l)  {\small $\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=l]  (b1) {\small $\langle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b1] (b2) {\small $B$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b2] (b3) {\small $\rangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=b3] (a1) {\small $\langle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $A$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $\rangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (r)  {\small $\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}$};
    % head
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of a2] (M) {\small $B$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (a2.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

$B$ has written $\langle A \rangle$ next to $\langle B \rangle$. Both descriptions are now on the tape.

\item Step 3: $B$ combines $\langle A \rangle$ and $\langle B \rangle$ into $\langle AB \rangle = \langle \textsc{Self} \rangle$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (l)  {\small $\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=l]  (a1) {\small $\langle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $A$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $B$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $\rangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (e1) {\small $\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=e1] (e2) {\small $\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=e2] (r)  {\small $\triangle$};
    % head at blank: machine halts
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of e1] (M) {\small $B$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (e1.north);
    % brace under <AB>
    \draw[decorate, decoration={brace, amplitude=5pt, mirror}]
        (a1.south west) -- (a4.south east)
        node[midway, below=6pt, font=\small] {$\langle\textsc{Self}\rangle$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}


$B$ halts. The tape contains $\langle AB \rangle = \langle\textsc{Self}\rangle$: \textsc{Self} has printed its own description.

\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example 2:}

Suppose for contradiction that $H$ is a TM that decides $A_{TM}$, i.e.,

\[
H(\langle M, x \rangle) = 
\begin{cases} 
\emph{accept} & \text{if } M \text{ accepts } x \\
\emph{reject} & \text{if } M \text{ does not accept } x
\end{cases}
\]

We construct a TM $B$ that takes input $\langle M \rangle$ and does the following:

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.6\textwidth}
$B$ = on input $\langle M \rangle$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Obtain $\langle B \rangle$ via the Recursion Theorem
    \item Run $H$ on $\langle B, \langle M \rangle \rangle$
    \item If $H$ accepts, \emph{reject}
    \item If $H$ rejects, \emph{accept}
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

Now we run $B$ on input $\langle B \rangle$:

\[
H(\langle B, \langle B \rangle \rangle) = \emph{accept}
\to B \text{ rejects } \langle B \rangle. \text{Contradiction.}
\]
\[
H(\langle B, \langle B \rangle \rangle) = \emph{reject}
\to B \text{ accepts } \langle B \rangle. \text{Contradiction.}
\]

In both cases we reach a contradiction. Therefore no such $H$ exists, 
and $A_{TM}$ is undecidable.

\section{Time Complexity}

\textbf{Example 1: }

Suppose we have an array like in the following example

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=l]  (a1) {\small $3$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $7$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $9$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $4$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a5] (a6) {\small $6$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a6] (a7) {\small $2$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a7] (a8) {\small $8$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a8] (a9) {\small $5$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

and we want to sort it in ascending order. One simple algorithm is \textsc{Selection Sort}:

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.6\textwidth}
\textsc{Selection Sort} = on input array $A$ of length $n$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item For $i = 1$ to $n$:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Find the smallest element in $A[i \dots n]$.
        \item Swap it with $A[i]$.
    \end{enumerate}
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

As we can see that in order to sort the number in the array, we need to find the smallest element in the unsorted portion of the array and swap it with the first element of the unsorted portion. 
This process is repeated until the entire array is sorted. For that array then we sort it in the following way:

\medskip

\noindent Pass 1: scan $A[1..9]$ to find minimum $= 1$, swap with $A[1]$:
\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm]              (a1) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $7$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $3$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $9$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $4$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a5] (a6) {\small $6$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a6] (a7) {\small $2$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a7] (a8) {\small $8$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a8] (a9) {\small $5$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\noindent Pass 2: scan $A[2..9]$ to find minimum $= 2$, swap with $A[2]$:
\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm]              (a1) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $2$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $3$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $9$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $4$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a5] (a6) {\small $6$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a6] (a7) {\small $7$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a7] (a8) {\small $8$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a8] (a9) {\small $5$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\noindent Pass 3: scan $A[3..9]$ to find minimum $= 3$, already in place:
\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm]              (a1) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $2$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $3$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $9$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $4$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a5] (a6) {\small $6$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a6] (a7) {\small $7$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a7] (a8) {\small $8$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a8] (a9) {\small $5$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\noindent Pass 4: scan $A[4..9]$ to find minimum $= 4$, swap with $A[4]$:
\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm]              (a1) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $2$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $3$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $4$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $9$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a5] (a6) {\small $6$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a6] (a7) {\small $7$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a7] (a8) {\small $8$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a8] (a9) {\small $5$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\noindent Pass 5: scan $A[5..9]$ to find minimum $= 5$, swap with $A[5]$:
\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm]              (a1) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $2$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $3$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $4$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $5$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a5] (a6) {\small $6$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a6] (a7) {\small $7$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a7] (a8) {\small $8$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a8] (a9) {\small $9$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\noindent Passes 6--8: elements $6, 7, 8$ are already in place; no swaps needed. The array is now fully sorted.

In total, selection sort performs $8$ passes (i.e.\ $n-1$ passes for $n=9$). In each pass $i$, it scans $n - i$ elements to find the minimum, giving a total number of comparisons of:

\[
(n-1) + (n-2) + \cdots + 1 = \frac{n(n-1)}{2} = \frac{9 \cdot 8}{2} = 36 \text{ comparisons.}
\]

From this example we can examine that if an array have $n$ elements, then selection sort will perform $\displaystyle\frac{n(n-1)}{2}$ comparisons in the worst case.
This is a quadratic function of $n$, so we say that selection sort has a time complexity. Then, we can conclude that running time generally depends on the size of the input. 

\textbf{Example 2:}

Suppose we have an array like in the following example

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm]              (a1) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $0$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $0$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $0$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Since in the array the number of $1$s and $0$s are not equal, we want to insert another $1$ to make them equal. The algorithm shifts all $0$s one position to the right to make room, then inserts a $1$ in the middle:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm]              (a1) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a1] (a2) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a2] (a3) {\small $1$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a3] (a4) {\small $0$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a4] (a5) {\small $0$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a5] (a6) {\small $0$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=a6] (a7) {\small $\ldots$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

To insert one element, we must shift $3$ elements (all the $0$s) one position to the right. In general, if the array has $n$ elements and we insert at position $i$, we must shift $n - i$ elements. In the worst case, inserting at the beginning requires shifting all $n$ elements.


Therefore, from the two examples above, we can see that the number of steps an algorithm takes depends on the size of the input $n$. We capture this relationship by defining $f(n)$ as the function that counts the number of steps the algorithm takes on an input of size $n$, and we call this the \emph{time complexity} of the algorithm. 

\subsection{Growth rate}

When analyzing algorithms, we are interested in how the running time grows as the input size $n$ increases. 
Rather than counting the exact number of steps, we focus on the dominant term, the part that grows the fastest and eventually overshadows all other terms.

\textbf{Example 1:}

Suppose we have an algorithm that takes $f(n) = 3n^2 + 2n + 1$ steps to complete on an input of size $n$.
As $n$ grows larger, we can observe how each term contributes to the total:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{c|c|c|c|c|c|c}
    $n$ & $3n^2$ & $\%$ & $2n$ & $\%$ & $1$ & $f(n)$ \\
    \hline
    1    & 3       & 50\%   & 2    & 33\%  & 1 & 6       \\
    10   & 300     & 93\%   & 20   & 6\%   & 1 & 321     \\
    100  & 30000   & 99.3\% & 200  & 0.7\% & 1 & 30201   \\
    1000 & 3000000 & 99.9\% & 2000 & 0.1\% & 1 & 3002001 \\
    \vdots & \vdots  & \vdots  & \vdots & \vdots  & \vdots & \vdots
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

As $n$ becomes large, the $3n^2$ term dominates the others. At $n = 1000$, it already accounts for $99.9\%$ of the total running time, while $2n$ shrinks to $0.1\%$ and the constant $1$ becomes completely negligible.
This suggests that for large $n$, the lower-order terms and constants have little effect on the overall growth of $f(n)$. What truly matters is the fastest growing term, which in this case is $n^2$.

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[
    xlabel={$n$},
    legend style={at={(1.02,1)}, anchor=north west, draw=none, fill=none},
    grid=none,
    width=11cm,
    height=8cm,
    ymode=log,
    xmode=log,
    xmin=1,
    xmax=1000
]
\addplot[mark=none, color=red!90!black] coordinates {
    (1,3) (10,300) (100,30000) (1000,3000000)
};
\addlegendentry{$3n^2$}

\addplot[mark=none, color=blue!90!black] coordinates {
    (1,2) (10,20) (100,200) (1000,2000)
};
\addlegendentry{$2n$}

\addplot[mark=none, color=green!90!black] coordinates {
    (1,1) (10,1) (100,1) (1000,1)
};
\addlegendentry{$1$}

\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{figure}

\subsection{Big $O$ Notation}

Big $O$ notation provides a way to describe the upper bound of an algorithm's growth rate, focusing on the dominant term and ignoring constants and lower-order terms.

Formally, 

\[
f(n) = O(g(n))
\;\leftrightarrow\;
\exists\, c > 0,\; \exists\, n_0
\;\text{s.t.}\;
\forall\, n \ge n_0,\;
f(n) \le c \cdot g(n).
\]

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = 4n + 3 \quad \land \quad g(n) = n
\]

To show that \( f(n) = O(g(n)) \), we need to find constants \( c \) and \( n_0 \) such that for all \( n \ge n_0 \), the inequality
$f(n) \le c \cdot g(n)$ holds.

Firstly, substituting the functions, we obtain

\[
4n + 3 \le c \cdot n.
\]

Choose \( c = 5 \). Then we need

\[
4n + 3 \le 5n.
\]

Subtracting \( 4n \) from both sides gives

\[
3 \le n.
\]

Thus, the inequality holds for all \( n \ge 3 \). Taking \( c = 5 \) and \( n_0 = 3 \), we conclude that \( f(n) \le c \cdot g(n) \) for all \( n \ge n_0 \).
Therefore

\[\boxed{f(n) = O(g(n))}\]


Comparing $f(n) = 4n + 3$ against $c \cdot g(n) = 5n$ for $n = 1, \ldots, 9$, with $c = 5$:

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{$n$} & \textbf{$f(n)$} & \textbf{$g(n)$} \\
\hline
1 & 7  & 5  \\
2 & 11 & 10 \\
3 & 15 & 15 \\
4 & 19 & 20 \\
5 & 23 & 25 \\
6 & 27 & 30 \\
7 & 31 & 35 \\
8 & 35 & 40 \\
9 & 39 & 45 \\
\vdots & \vdots & \vdots \\ 
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\newpage
Intuitively, 

\begin{figure}[h]
\centering
\begin{tikzpicture}
\begin{axis}[
    xlabel={$n$},
    legend style={at={(1.02,1)}, anchor=north west, draw=none, fill=none},
    grid=none,
    width=10cm,
    height=7cm,
    ymin=0,
    xmin=1,
    xmax=9
]
\addplot[mark=none, color=red!90!black] coordinates {
    (1,7) (2,11) (3,15) (4,19) (5,23) (6,27) (7,31) (8,35) (9,39)
};
\addlegendentry{$f(n)$}

\addplot[mark=none, color=blue!90!black] coordinates {
    (1,5) (2,10) (3,15) (4,20) (5,25) (6,30) (7,35) (8,40) (9,45)
};
\addlegendentry{$g(n)$}

\addplot[dashed, mark=none, color=gray, forget plot] coordinates {
    (3,0) (3,15)
};
\node[gray] at (axis cs:3,0) [below] {$n_0=3$};

\end{axis}
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{figure}

We claim that both functions grow linearly, and that $f(n)$ is upper-bounded by $c \cdot g(n)$ for all sufficiently large $n$.

\subsection{Standard Functions}

\begin{enumerate}


\item Constant: $f(n) = c$

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = 7 \quad \land \quad g(n) = 1
\]

We want to show that $f(n) = O(g(n))$.

We need constants $c_1$ and $n_0$ such that

\[
7 \le c_1 \cdot 1
\]

Choose $c_1 = 7$. The inequality holds for all $n \ge 1$.

As a result,

\[
\boxed{f(n) = O(1)}
\]


\item Logarithmic: $f(n) = \log n$

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = 3\log n + 2 \quad \land \quad g(n) = \log n
\]

We need

\[
3\log n + 2 \le c \log n
\]

Choose $c = 5$. Then:

\[
3\log n + 2 \le 5\log n
\]

This holds for all $n \ge 2$.

We conclude that,

\[
\boxed{f(n) = O(\log n)}
\]


\item Linear: $f(n) = n$

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = 4n + 3 \quad \land \quad g(n) = n
\]

We need

\[
4n + 3 \le c n
\]

Choose $c = 5$. Then:

\[
4n + 3 \le 5n
\]

This holds for all $n \ge 3$.

Accordingly,

\[
\boxed{f(n) = O(n)}
\]


\item Linearithmic: $f(n) = n\log n$

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = 2n\log n + n \quad \land \quad g(n) = n\log n
\]

We need

\[
2n\log n + n \le c n\log n
\]

Choose $c = 3$. For $n \ge 2$, this holds.

In conclusion,

\[
\boxed{f(n) = O(n\log n)}
\]


\item Quadratic: $f(n) = n^2$

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = 5n^2 + 2n + 1 \quad \land \quad g(n) = n^2
\]

We need

\[
5n^2 + 2n + 1 \le c n^2
\]

Choose $c = 8$. This holds for all $n \ge 1$.

Hence,

\[
\boxed{f(n) = O(n^2)}
\]


\item Cubic: $f(n) = n^3$

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = n^3 + 10n^2 \quad \land \quad g(n) = n^3
\]

We need

\[
n^3 + 10n^2 \le c n^3
\]

Choose $c = 11$. This holds for all $n \ge 1$.

Therefore,

\[
\boxed{f(n) = O(n^3)}
\]

\item Exponential: $f(n) = 2^n$

\textbf{Example:}

Suppose

\[
f(n) = 3 \cdot 2^n + n \quad \land \quad g(n) = 2^n
\]

We need

\[
3 \cdot 2^n + n \le c \cdot 2^n
\]

Choose $c = 4$. For all $n \ge 1$, the inequality holds.

Thus,

\[
\boxed{f(n) = O(2^n)}
\]

\end{enumerate}

Specially, we have the following growth rates:

\[O(1) < O(\log n) < O(n) < O(n \log n) < O(n^2) < O(n^3) < O(2^n)\]

\[
\begin{array}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
\multicolumn{7}{|c|}{g(n)} \\
\hline
n & \log_2 n & n & n\log_2 n & n^2 & n^3 & 2^n \\
\hline
1  & 0 & 1  & 0   & 1    & 1     & 2 \\
2  & 1 & 2  & 2   & 4    & 8     & 4 \\
4  & 2 & 4  & 8   & 16   & 64    & 16 \\
8  & 3 & 8  & 24  & 64   & 512   & 256 \\
16 & 4 & 16 & 64  & 256  & 4096  & 65536 \\
32 & 5 & 32 & 160 & 1024 & 32768 & 4294967296 \\
\hline
\end{array}
\]

\section{Analyzing Algorithms}

\textbf{Example 1:}

Suppose we have a Turing machine $M$ that decides the language

\[
A = \{\, 1^k 0^k \mid k \ge 1 \,\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$M$ on input $w$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Scan the input to check whether $w \in 1^*0^*$.
    If not, \emph{reject}.

    \item Repeat until no unread symbols remain:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Scan from left to find the leftmost unread symbol and replace it with $\triangle$.
        \item Scan from right to find the rightmost unread symbol and match it.
        \item If the symbols do not match, \emph{reject}.
        \item Replace the matched symbol with $\triangle$.
    \end{enumerate}

    \item If any unread symbols remain, \emph{reject}; otherwise, \emph{accept}.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

Initial tape:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (M) {\small $M$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 1: Read leftmost $1$, mark it with $\triangle$, move right to find rightmost unread symbol:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c3] (M) {\small $M$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (c3.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 2: Rightmost unread is $0$, matches remembered symbol $0$. Mark it with $\triangle$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c3] (M) {\small $M$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (c3.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 3: Move back left to next unread symbol $1$, mark it, move right to rightmost unread $0$, match:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c2] (M) {\small $M$};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (c2.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 4: All symbols matched. Head sees only $\triangle$ and \scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}. TM halts and accepts.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c1] (M) {\textbf{ACCEPT}};
    \draw[->] (M.south) -- (c1.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}


From this algorithm, we can see that the running time is $O(n^2)$, where $n$ is the length of the input string.
In the worst case, the input is of the form $1^k 0^k$ with $n = 2k$, so the algorithm performs $k$ iterations,
each requiring a full scan of the tape taking $O(n)$ time. Since $k = \displaystyle\frac{n}{2}$,
we multiply by $n$ to obtain the total number of steps across all iterations, as each iteration
costs $n$ steps:

\[
k \cdot n = \frac{n}{2} \cdot n = \frac{n^2}{2} = O(n^2).
\]


\textbf{Example 2:}

Suppose we have a Turing machine $T$ that decides the language

\[
A = \{\, 1^k 0^k \mid k \ge 1 \,\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$T$ on input $w$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Scan the input to check whether $w \in 1^*0^*$.
    If not, \emph{reject}.

    \item Repeat until no unread symbols remain:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item Scan left to right and mark every \emph{other} unread $1$ with $\triangle$.
        \item Scan right to left and mark every \emph{other} unread $0$ with $\triangle$.
        \item If the number of remaining $1$s and $0$s are unequal, \emph{reject}.
    \end{enumerate}

    \item If any unread symbols remain, \emph{reject}; otherwise, \emph{accept}.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

Initial tape (with $k = 4$, so $n = 8$):

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c4] (c5) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c5] (c6) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c6] (c7) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c7] (c8) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c8] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (T) {\small $T$};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 1 (Iteration 1): Mark every other $1$ and every other $0$ with $\triangle$, halving the unread symbols:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c4] (c5) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c5] (c6) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c6] (c7) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c7] (c8) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c8] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (T) {\small $T$};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 2 (Iteration 2): Mark every other remaining $1$ and $0$, halving again:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c4] (c5) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c5] (c6) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c6] (c7) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c7] (c8) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c8] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (T) {\small $T$};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 3 (Iteration 3): Mark the last remaining $1$ and $0$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c4] (c5) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c5] (c6) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c6] (c7) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c7] (c8) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c8] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (T) {\small $T$};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 4: All symbols matched. TM halts and accepts.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c4] (c5) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c5] (c6) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c6] (c7) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c7] (c8) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c8] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c3] (T) {\textbf{ACCEPT}};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c3.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

The running time of this algorithm is $O(n \log n)$, where $n$ is the length of the input string.
Consider the worst case where the input is of the form $1^k 0^k$ with $n = 2k$. Since each iteration
marks half of the remaining symbols, the number of iterations is $O(\log n)$, and each iteration
scans the full tape in $O(n)$ time. For example, with $n = 8$:

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{Iteration} & \textbf{Remaining symbols} & \textbf{Marked this round} \\
\hline
1 & 8 & 4 $\rightarrow$ 4 left \\
2 & 4 & 2 $\rightarrow$ 2 left \\
3 & 2 & 1 $\rightarrow$ 1 left \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

\noindent Since the symbols are halved each iteration, the total number of iterations is $\log_2 n$.
Therefore, the total running time is:

\[
O(\log n) \cdot O(n) = O(n \log n).
\]

\subsection{TIME Complexity Classes}

Formally, let $ t : \mathbb{N} \to \mathbb{N} $ be a function. The time complexity class $\mathbf{TIME}(t(n))$ is defined as the collection of all languages decidable by an $O(t(n))$ deterministic Turing machine:

\[ \mathbf{TIME}(t(n)) = \{ L \mid L \text{ is decided by an } O(t(n)) \text{DTM} \} \]


\textbf{Example:}

Suppose we have a Turing machine $T$ that decides the language

\[
L = \{\, x \mid x = x^{\mathcal{R}},\ x \in \{0,1\}^* \,\}.
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$T$ on input $x$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item If the input is empty or a single symbol, \emph{accept}.
    \item Scan to the leftmost unread symbol, remember it, and mark it with $\triangle$.
    \item Scan to the rightmost unread symbol and compare it to the remembered symbol.
    If they differ, \emph{reject}; otherwise mark it with $\triangle$.
    \item If any unread symbols remain, go to step 2; otherwise \emph{accept}.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

Initial tape (with $x = 0110$, so $n = 4$):

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {0};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (T) {\small $T$};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 1 (Iteration 1): Compare outermost symbols $0$ and $0$, they match, mark both with $\triangle$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {1};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (T) {\small $T$};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 2 (Iteration 2): Compare next outermost symbols $1$ and $1$, they match, mark both with $\triangle$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c0] (T) {\small $T$};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c0.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Step 3: No unread symbols remain. TM halts and accepts.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=0.8cm]
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm] (c0) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c0] (c1) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c1] (c2) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c2] (c3) {$\triangle$};
    \node[draw, minimum size=0.8cm, right of=c3] (c4) {\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}};
    \node[right of=c4] {\ldots};
    \node[draw, minimum width=0.8cm, minimum height=0.8cm, above=1.2cm of c1] (T) {\textbf{ACCEPT}};
    \draw[->] (T.south) -- (c1.north);
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

Each iteration scans the full tape in $O(n)$ time to locate and compare the outermost 
unread symbols, marking one pair per iteration. Since the input has $n$ symbols and 
we mark two per iteration, there are $n/2$ iterations in total. For example, with $n = 4$:

\begin{table}[h]
\centering
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
\hline
\textbf{Iteration} & \textbf{Remaining symbols} & \textbf{Marked this round} \\
\hline
1 & 4 & 1 pair $\rightarrow$ 2 left \\
2 & 2 & 1 pair $\rightarrow$ 0 left \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{table}

Since one pair is marked per iteration, the total number of iterations is $n/2$.
Therefore, the total running time is:

\[
O(n) \cdot O(n/2) = O(n^2).
\]

Hence $L \in \mathbf{TIME}(n^2)$.

\section{The Class P}

The class $\mathbf{P}$ consists of all languages decidable by a DTM in polynomial time:

\[\mathbf{P} = \bigcup_{k \geq 1} \mathbf{TIME}(n^k)\]

\textbf{Example 1:}

The language $T = \{ x  \mid x = x^R,  x \in \{0,1\}^*  \}$ is in $\mathbf{P}$.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$T$ on input $x$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item If the input is empty or a single symbol, \emph{accept}.
    \item Scan to the leftmost unread symbol, remember it, and mark it with $\triangle$.
    \item Scan to the rightmost unread symbol and compare it to the remembered symbol.
    If they differ, \emph{reject}; otherwise mark it with $\triangle$.
    \item If any unread symbols remain, go to step 2; otherwise \emph{accept}.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

As shown in the previous example, this algorithm runs in $O(n^2)$ time, which is polynomial. Therefore, $T \in \mathbf{P}$.

\textbf{Example 2:}

\[\text{PATH} = \{ \langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle \mid G \text{ is a directed graph with a path from } w_0 \text{ to } w_5 \} \text{ is in } \mathbf{P} \]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[->, >=stealth, node distance=3cm]

  \node[state] (w0) {$w_0$};
  \node[state] (w1) [above right of=w0] {$w_1$};
  \node[state] (w2) [below right of=w0] {$w_2$};
  \node[state] (w3) [right of=w1] {$w_3$};
  \node[state] (w4) [right of=w2] {$w_4$};
  \node[state] (w5) [below right of=w3] {$w_5$};

  \draw (w0) edge (w1);
  \draw (w0) edge (w2);
  \draw (w1) edge (w3);
  \draw (w1) edge (w4);
  \draw (w2) edge (w4);
  \draw (w2) edge (w3);
  \draw (w3) edge (w5);
  \draw (w4) edge (w5);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

where $G = (V, E)$ with:

\[
V = \{w_0, w_1, w_2, w_3, w_4, w_5\}
\]

\[
E = \{(w_0,w_1),\ (w_0,w_2),\ (w_1,w_3),\ (w_1,w_4),\ (w_2,w_4),\ (w_2,w_3),\ (w_3,w_5),\ (w_4,w_5)\}
\]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$M$ on input $\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Mark node $w_0$.
    \item Scan all edges $(u, v)$ in $G$: if $u$ is marked and $v$ is not, mark $v$.
    \item If any new node was marked in step 2, repeat step 2.
    \item If $w_5$ is marked, \emph{accept}; otherwise \emph{reject}.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

We show $\text{PATH} \in \mathbf{P}$ by constructing a polynomial-time decider.
A node $v$ is marked if and only if there exists a directed path from $w_0$ to $v$. Hence $M$ accepts if and only if
$\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle \in \text{PATH}$.

Let $G = (V, E)$ where $V$ is the set of nodes and $E$ is the set of directed edges. Let $n = |V|$ and $m = |E|$.
Each iteration of step 2 marks at least one new node, so the loop runs at most $n$ times. Each iteration scans all $m$ edges. Since each ordered pair of nodes admits at most one edge, we have $|E| \leq |V|^2$, i.e.\ $m \leq n^2$, and therefore:

\[
n \cdot m \leq n \cdot n^2 = n^3
\]

and thus:

\[
O(n \cdot m) \subseteq O(n \cdot n^2) = O(n^3)
\]

which is polynomial in $|\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle|$. Therefore, $M$ runs in polynomial time and $\text{PATH} \in \mathbf{P}$.

\section{Nondeterministic Polynomial Time (NP)}

Formally, let $t : \mathbb{N} \to \mathbb{N}$ be a function. The \emph{nondeterministic time complexity class} $\mathbf{NTIME}(t(n))$ is defined as:

\[
\mathbf{NTIME}(t(n)) = \{ L \mid L \text{ is a language decided by a NTM in time } t(n) \}
\]

Then $\mathbf{NP}$ is defined as the union over all polynomial time bounds:

\[
\mathbf{NP} = \bigcup_{k \in \mathbb{N}} \mathbf{NTIME}(n^k)
\]

The class $\mathbf{NP}$ can equivalently be characterized using a \emph{verifier}: a deterministic Turing machine that checks whether a proposed solution is correct. Formally, a language $L \subseteq \Sigma^*$ is in $\mathbf{NP}$ if and only if there exists a deterministic Turing machine $V$ that runs in polynomial time and a polynomial $p : \mathbb{N} \to \mathbb{N}$ such that for all $x \in \Sigma^*$:

\[
x \in L \leftrightarrow \exists c \in \Sigma^* \text{ with } |c| \leq p(|x|) \text{ such that } V(x,c) = 1
\]

$V$ is called the \emph{verifier} for $L$, and $c$ is called the \emph{certificate} for $x$.

Intuitively, a nondeterministic machine can be thought of as \emph{guessing} a certificate $c$ and then verifying it. 


\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[
  level distance=1.5cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=5.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=3cm},
  level 3/.style={sibling distance=1.5cm},
  every node/.style={circle, draw, minimum size=0.7cm},
  accept/.style={circle, draw, double, minimum size=0.7cm},
  reject/.style={circle, draw, fill=gray!30, minimum size=0.7cm}
]

\node {$x$}
  child { node {$c_1$}
    child { node[reject] {$0$} }
    child { node {$c_{i}$}
      child { node[reject] {$0$} }
      child { node[accept] {$1$} }
    }
  }
  child { node {$c_2$}
    child { node {$c_{j}$}
      child { node[reject] {$0$} }
      child { node[reject] {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$c_{k}$}
      child { node[reject] {$0$} }
      child { node[accept] {$1$} }
    }
  };

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

Each path from the root $x$ to a leaf represents one possible guess of a certificate. 
The machine \emph{accepts} if at least one branch reaches an accept state $1$; it \emph{rejects} if all branches reach a reject state $0$. 
The depth of the tree is polynomial in $|x|$.

\subsection{Hamiltonian Path}

A \emph{Hamiltonian path} from $w_0$ to $w_5$ is a directed path that visits every node in $V$ exactly once.

\textbf{Example: }

\[\text{HAMPATH} = \{ \langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle \mid G \text{ is a directed graph with a Hamiltonian path from } w_0 \text{ to } w_5 \} \]

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[->, >=stealth, node distance=3cm]

  \node[state] (w0) {$w_0$};
  \node[state] (w1) [above right of=w0] {$w_1$};
  \node[state] (w2) [below right of=w0] {$w_2$};
  \node[state] (w3) [right of=w1] {$w_3$};
  \node[state] (w4) [right of=w2] {$w_4$};
  \node[state] (w5) [below right of=w3] {$w_5$};

  \draw (w0) edge (w1);
  \draw (w0) edge (w2);
  \draw (w1) edge (w3);
  \draw (w1) edge (w4);
  \draw (w2) edge (w4);
  \draw (w2) edge (w3);
  \draw (w3) edge (w5);
  \draw (w4) edge (w5);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

where $G = (V, E)$ with:

\[
V = \{w_0, w_1, w_2, w_3, w_4, w_5\}
\]

\[
E = \{(w_0,w_1),\ (w_0,w_2),\ (w_1,w_3),\ (w_1,w_4),\ (w_2,w_4),\ (w_2,w_3),\ (w_3,w_5),\ (w_4,w_5)\}
\]

A \emph{Hamiltonian path} from $w_0$ to $w_5$ is a directed path that visits every node in $V$ exactly once.
For this graph, the path $w_0 \to w_1 \to w_4 \to w_3 \to w_5$ visits only 5 of the 6 nodes and is therefore \emph{not} Hamiltonian, whereas $w_0 \to w_2 \to w_3 \to w_5$ also fails. Indeed, one can check that no path visits all six nodes, so $\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle \notin \text{HAMPATH}$.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$N$ on input $\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Nondeterministically select a permutation $\pi = (v_1, v_2, \ldots, v_n)$ of $V$.
    \item Check that $v_1 = w_0$ and $v_n = w_5$.
    \item For each $i \in \{1, \ldots, n-1\}$, check that $(v_i, v_{i+1}) \in E$.
    \item If all checks pass, $1$; otherwise $0$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

We show $\text{HAMPATH} \in \mathbf{NP}$ by constructing a polynomial-time nondeterministic Turing machine $N$.
A permutation $\pi$ passes all checks if and only if it is a Hamiltonian path from $w_0$ to $w_5$. Hence $N$ accepts $\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle$ on some branch if and only if $\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle \in \text{HAMPATH}$.
Let $n = |V|$ and $m = |E|$. The running time of each step is:

\begin{enumerate}

    \item Step 1: Writes down $n$ node labels in $O(n)$ time.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.5cm}]
  \node {Write $n$ labels}
    child { node {$v_1 = w_0$} }
    child { node {$v_2 = w_1$} }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$v_n = w_5$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 2: Performs two comparisons in $O(1)$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=4cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=2cm}]
  \node {Check endpoints}
    child { node {$v_1 = w_0$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$v_n = w_5$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 3: Performs $n - 1$ edge-membership checks; with an adjacency-matrix representation each check is $O(1)$, giving $O(n)$ total.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.8cm}]
  \node {Check $n-1$ edges}
    child { node {$(v_1,v_2) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$(v_2,v_3) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$(v_{n-1},v_n) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 4: Performs a single check in $O(1)$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=4cm}]
  \node {All checks passed?}
    child { node {$1$} }
    child { node {$0$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\end{enumerate}

Thus the entire computation runs in $O(n)$ time, which is polynomial in $|\langle G, w_0, w_5 \rangle|$.
A \emph{certificate} for membership is the sequence $(v_1, \ldots, v_n)$ itself, whose length is $O(n)$. A deterministic verifier checks the three conditions in Steps~2--3 in $O(n)$ time, confirming that $\text{HAMPATH} \in \mathbf{NP}$.

\subsection{Vertext Cover}

A \emph{vertex cover} is a subset $S \subseteq V$ such that every edge in $G$ has at least one endpoint in $S$.

\textbf{Example: }

Consider $G = (V, E)$ with:

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=3cm]

  \node[state] (w0) {$w_0$};
  \node[state] (w1) [above right of=w0] {$w_1$};
  \node[state] (w2) [below right of=w0] {$w_2$};
  \node[state] (w3) [right of=w1] {$w_3$};
  \node[state] (w4) [right of=w2] {$w_4$};
  \node[state] (w5) [below right of=w3] {$w_5$};

  \draw (w0) -- (w1);
  \draw (w0) -- (w2);
  \draw (w1) -- (w3);
  \draw (w1) -- (w4);
  \draw (w2) -- (w4);
  \draw (w3) -- (w5);
  \draw (w4) -- (w5);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\[
V = \{w_0, w_1, w_2, w_3, w_4, w_5\}, \quad k = 3
\]

\[
E = \{(w_0,w_1),\ (w_0,w_2),\ (w_1,w_3),\ (w_1,w_4),\ (w_2,w_4),\ (w_3,w_5),\ (w_4,w_5)\}
\]

Taking $S = \{w_1, w_2, w_4\}$, we verify that $S$ covers every edge:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_1)$ & covered by $w_1 \in S$ \\
$(w_0, w_2)$ & covered by $w_2 \in S$ \\
$(w_1, w_3)$ & covered by $w_1 \in S$ \\
$(w_1, w_4)$ & covered by $w_1 \in S$ \\
$(w_2, w_4)$ & covered by $w_2 \in S$ \\
$(w_3, w_5)$ & covered by $w_4 \notin S$, not covered by $w_3 \notin S$ \\
$(w_4, w_5)$ & covered by $w_4 \in S$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Since $(w_3, w_5)$ is not covered, $S = \{w_1, w_2, w_4\}$ is \emph{not} a valid vertex cover. Taking instead $S = \{w_1, w_2, w_4\}$ with $w_3$ replaced, we try $S = \{w_1, w_3, w_4\}$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_1)$ & covered by $w_1 \in S$ \\
$(w_0, w_2)$ & not covered: $w_0 \notin S,\ w_2 \notin S$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Hence $\langle G, 3 \rangle \notin \text{VERTEX-COVER}$ for these choices. Taking $S = \{w_0, w_1, w_4\}$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_1)$ & covered by $w_0 \in S$ \\
$(w_0, w_2)$ & covered by $w_0 \in S$ \\
$(w_1, w_3)$ & covered by $w_1 \in S$ \\
$(w_1, w_4)$ & covered by $w_1 \in S$ \\
$(w_2, w_4)$ & covered by $w_4 \in S$ \\
$(w_3, w_5)$ & not covered: $w_3 \notin S,\ w_5 \notin S$ \\
$(w_4, w_5)$ & covered by $w_4 \in S$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

No subset of size $k = 3$ covers all edges, so $\langle G, 3 \rangle \notin \text{VERTEX-COVER}$. However, taking $k = 4$ with $S = \{w_0, w_1, w_4, w_3\}$ covers all edges, so $\langle G, 4 \rangle \in \text{VERTEX-COVER}$.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$N$ on input $\langle G, k \rangle$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Nondeterministically select a subset $S \subseteq V$ with $|S| = k$.
    \item For each edge $(u, v) \in E$, check that $u \in S$ or $v \in S$.
    \item If all checks pass, $1$; otherwise $0$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

We show $\text{VERTEX-COVER} \in \mathbf{NP}$ by constructing a polynomial-time nondeterministic Turing machine $N$.
A subset $S$ passes all checks if and only if it is a vertex cover of size $k$. Hence $N$ accepts $\langle G, k \rangle$ on some branch if and only if $\langle G, k \rangle \in \text{VERTEX-COVER}$.
Let $n = |V|$ and $m = |E|$. The running time of each step is:

\begin{enumerate}

    \item Step 1: Selects $k$ nodes from $V$ in $O(n)$ time.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.5cm}]
  \node {Select $k$ nodes}
    child { node {$w_0$} }
    child { node {$w_1$} }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$w_n$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 2: Performs $m$ edge checks; each membership check is $O(1)$, giving $O(m)$ total.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.8cm}]
  \node {Check $m$ edges}
    child { node {$(w_0,w_1) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$(w_1,w_3) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$(w_4,w_5) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 3: Performs a single check in $O(1)$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=4cm}]
  \node {All edges covered?}
    child { node {$1$} }
    child { node {$0$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\end{enumerate}

Since each pair of nodes admits at most one edge, we have $|E| \leq \displaystyle\frac{n(n-1)}{2}$, and therefore:

\[
n \cdot m \leq n \cdot \frac{n(n-1)}{2} = \frac{n^2(n-1)}{2}
\]

and thus:

\[
O(n \cdot m) \subseteq O\!\left(\frac{n^2(n-1)}{2}\right) = O(n^3)
\]

which is polynomial in $|\langle G, k \rangle|$. A \emph{certificate} for membership is the subset $S$ itself, whose length is $O(n)$. A deterministic verifier checks all edges in $O(m)$ time, confirming that $\text{VERTEX-COVER} \in \mathbf{NP}$.

\subsection{Clique}

A \emph{clique} is a subset $C \subseteq V$ such that every two nodes in $C$ are connected by an edge, i.e.:
\[
\forall u, v \in C, u \neq v : (u, v) \in E
\]

\textbf{Example: }

Consider $G = (V, E)$ with:

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=3cm]

  \node[state] (w0) {$w_0$};
  \node[state] (w1) [above right of=w0] {$w_1$};
  \node[state] (w2) [below right of=w0] {$w_2$};
  \node[state] (w3) [right of=w1] {$w_3$};
  \node[state] (w4) [right of=w2] {$w_4$};
  \node[state] (w5) [below right of=w3] {$w_5$};

  \draw (w0) -- (w1);
  \draw (w0) -- (w2);
  \draw (w1) -- (w2);
  \draw (w1) -- (w3);
  \draw (w2) -- (w4);
  \draw (w3) -- (w4);
  \draw (w3) -- (w5);
  \draw (w4) -- (w5);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\[
V = \{w_0, w_1, w_2, w_3, w_4, w_5\}, \quad k = 3
\]

\[
E = \{(w_0,w_1),\ (w_0,w_2),\ (w_1,w_2),\ (w_1,w_3),\ (w_2,w_4),\ (w_3,w_4),\ (w_3,w_5),\ (w_4,w_5)\}
\]

Taking $C = \{w_0, w_1, w_2\}$, we verify that every pair in $C$ is connected:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_1)$ & $(w_0, w_1) \in E$ \\
$(w_0, w_2)$ & $(w_0, w_2) \in E$ \\
$(w_1, w_2)$ & $(w_1, w_2) \in E$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

All pairs are connected, so $C = \{w_0, w_1, w_2\}$ is a valid clique of size $k = 3$, and thus $\langle G, 3 \rangle \in \text{CLIQUE}$.
Taking $C = \{w_3, w_4, w_5\}$, we verify:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_3, w_4)$ & $(w_3, w_4) \in E$ \\
$(w_3, w_5)$ & $(w_3, w_5) \in E$ \\
$(w_4, w_5)$ & $(w_4, w_5) \in E$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

This is also a valid clique of size $k = 3$. However, no clique of size $k = 4$ exists, so $\langle G, 4 \rangle \notin \text{CLIQUE}$.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$N$ on input $\langle G, k \rangle$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Nondeterministically select a subset $C \subseteq V$ with $|C| = k$.
    \item For each pair $u, v \in C$ with $u \neq v$, check that $(u, v) \in E$.
    \item If all checks pass, $1$; otherwise $0$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

We show $\text{CLIQUE} \in \mathbf{NP}$ by constructing a polynomial-time nondeterministic Turing machine $N$.
A subset $C$ passes all checks if and only if it is a clique of size $k$. Hence $N$ accepts $\langle G, k \rangle$ on some branch if and only if $\langle G, k \rangle \in \text{CLIQUE}$.
Let $n = |V|$ and $m = |E|$. The running time of each step is:

\begin{enumerate}

    \item Step 1: Selects $k$ nodes from $V$ in $O(n)$ time.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.5cm}]
  \node {Select $k$ nodes}
    child { node {$w_0$} }
    child { node {$w_1$} }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$w_n$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 2: Performs $\binom{k}{2}$ pair checks; each membership check is $O(1)$, giving $O(k^2)$ total.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.8cm}]
  \node {Check $\binom{k}{2}$ pairs}
    child { node {$(w_0,w_1) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$(w_0,w_2) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$(w_1,w_2) \in E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 3: Performs a single check in $O(1)$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=4cm}]
  \node {All pairs connected?}
    child { node {$1$} }
    child { node {$0$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\end{enumerate}

Since $k \leq n$, the number of pairs checked is $ \displaystyle\binom{k}{2} \leq \binom{n}{2} = \frac{n(n-1)}{2}$, and therefore:

\[
n \cdot \frac{n(n-1)}{2} = \frac{n^2(n-1)}{2}
\]

and thus:

\[
O\!\left(\frac{n^2(n-1)}{2}\right) = O(n^3)
\]

which is polynomial in $|\langle G, k \rangle|$. A \emph{certificate} for membership is the subset $C$ itself, whose length is $O(n)$. A deterministic verifier checks all pairs in $O(k^2) \subseteq O(n^2)$ time, confirming that $\text{CLIQUE} \in \mathbf{NP}$.

\subsection{Independent Set}

A \emph{independent set} is a subset $I \subseteq V$ such that no two nodes in $I$ are connected by an edge, i.e.:
\[
\forall u, v \in I, u \neq v : (u, v) \notin E
\]

\textbf{Example: }

Consider $G = (V, E)$ with:

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{tikzpicture}[node distance=3cm]

  \node[state] (w0) {$w_0$};
  \node[state] (w1) [above right of=w0] {$w_1$};
  \node[state] (w2) [below right of=w0] {$w_2$};
  \node[state] (w3) [right of=w1] {$w_3$};
  \node[state] (w4) [right of=w2] {$w_4$};
  \node[state] (w5) [below right of=w3] {$w_5$};

  \draw (w0) -- (w1);
  \draw (w0) -- (w2);
  \draw (w1) -- (w2);
  \draw (w1) -- (w3);
  \draw (w2) -- (w4);
  \draw (w3) -- (w4);
  \draw (w3) -- (w5);
  \draw (w4) -- (w5);

\end{tikzpicture}
}
\end{center}

\[
V = \{w_0, w_1, w_2, w_3, w_4, w_5\}, \quad k = 3
\]

\[
E = \{(w_0,w_1),\ (w_0,w_2),\ (w_1,w_2),\ (w_1,w_3),\ (w_2,w_4),\ (w_3,w_4),\ (w_3,w_5),\ (w_4,w_5)\}
\]

Taking $I = \{w_0, w_3, w_4\}$, we verify that no two nodes in $I$ are connected:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_3)$ & $(w_0, w_3) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
$(w_0, w_4)$ & $(w_0, w_4) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
$(w_3, w_4)$ & $(w_3, w_4) \in E$ \quad edge exists! \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Since $(w_3, w_4) \in E$, $I = \{w_0, w_3, w_4\}$ is \emph{not} a valid independent set. Taking instead $I = \{w_0, w_3, w_5\}$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_3)$ & $(w_0, w_3) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
$(w_0, w_5)$ & $(w_0, w_5) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
$(w_3, w_5)$ & $(w_3, w_5) \in E$ \quad edge exists! \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Since $(w_3, w_5) \in E$, $I = \{w_0, w_3, w_5\}$ is also \emph{not} valid. Taking instead $I = \{w_0, w_4, w_5\}$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_4)$ & $(w_0, w_4) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
