$(w_0, w_5)$ & $(w_0, w_5) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
$(w_4, w_5)$ & $(w_4, w_5) \in E$ \quad edge exists! \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Taking instead $I = \{w_0, w_3, w_4\}$ fails, so we try $I = \{w_0, w_5, w_2\}$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_5)$ & $(w_0, w_5) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
$(w_0, w_2)$ & $(w_0, w_2) \in E$ \quad edge exists! \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Taking $I = \{w_0, w_5, w_3\}$ fails as shown. However, taking $I = \{w_0, w_4, w_3\}$ fails too. We find that $I = \{w_2, w_3, w_5\}$ is not valid either. Taking $I = \{w_0, w_5\}$ with $k = 2$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(w_0, w_5)$ & $(w_0, w_5) \notin E$ \quad no edge \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

So $\langle G, 2 \rangle \in \text{INDEPENDENT-SET}$. No independent set of size $k = 3$ exists in this graph, so $\langle G, 3 \rangle \notin \text{INDEPENDENT-SET}$.

\begin{center}
\fbox{
\begin{minipage}{0.7\textwidth}
$N$ on input $\langle G, k \rangle$:
\begin{enumerate}
    \item Nondeterministically select a subset $I \subseteq V$ with $|I| = k$.
    \item For each pair $u, v \in I$ with $u \neq v$, check that $(u, v) \notin E$.
    \item If all checks pass, $1$; otherwise $0$.
\end{enumerate}
\end{minipage}
}
\end{center}

We show $\text{INDEPENDENT-SET} \in \mathbf{NP}$ by constructing a polynomial-time nondeterministic Turing machine $N$.
A subset $I$ passes all checks if and only if it is an independent set of size $k$. Hence $N$ accepts $\langle G, k \rangle$ on some branch if and only if $\langle G, k \rangle \in \text{INDEPENDENT-SET}$.
Let $n = |V|$ and $m = |E|$. The running time of each step is:

\begin{enumerate}

    \item Step 1: Selects $k$ nodes from $V$ in $O(n)$ time.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.5cm}]
  \node {Select $k$ nodes}
    child { node {$w_0$} }
    child { node {$w_1$} }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$w_n$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 2: Performs $\binom{k}{2}$pair checks; each membership check is $O(1)$, giving $O(k^2)$ total.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=3.5cm},
  level 2/.style={sibling distance=1.8cm}]
  \node {Check $\binom{k}{2}$ pairs}
    child { node {$(w_0,w_3) \notin E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$(w_0,w_4) \notin E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    }
    child { node {$\cdots$} }
    child { node {$(w_3,w_4) \notin E$?}
      child { node {$1$} }
      child { node {$0$} }
    };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

    \item Step 3: Performs a single check in $O(1)$.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}[level distance=1.2cm,
  level 1/.style={sibling distance=4cm}]
  \node {No pairs connected?}
    child { node {$1$} }
    child { node {$0$} };
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\end{enumerate}

Since $k \leq n$, the number of pairs checked is $\displaystyle\binom{k}{2} \leq \binom{n}{2}$, and therefore:

\[
n \cdot \frac{n(n-1)}{2} = \frac{n^2(n-1)}{2}
\]

and thus:

\[
O\!\left(\frac{n^2(n-1)}{2}\right) = O(n^3)
\]

which is polynomial in $|\langle G, k \rangle|$. A \emph{certificate} for membership is the subset $I$ itself, whose length is $O(n)$. A deterministic verifier checks all pairs in $O(k^2) \subseteq O(n^2)$ time, confirming that $\text{INDEPENDENT-SET} \in \mathbf{NP}$.

\section{NP-Complete}

A language $Y$ is \emph{NP-complete} if it satisfies two conditions:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $Y \in \mathbf{NP}$, and
    \item every $X \in \mathbf{NP}$ is polynomial-time reducible to $Y$, i.e.\ $X \leq_P Y$.
\end{enumerate}

Formally:

\[
\text{NP-complete} = \{ Y \in \mathbf{NP} \mid \forall X \in \mathbf{NP}: X \leq_P Y \}
\]

If $Y$ is NP-complete and $Y \in \mathbf{P}$, then $\mathbf{P} = \mathbf{NP}$.

\subsection{Boolean Satisfiability (SAT)}

A \emph{Boolean formula} is an expression over Boolean variables $x_1, x_2, \ldots, x_n$ using the operators:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\cdot$ (AND), true if both sides are true
    \item $+$ (OR), true if at least one side is true
    \item $\bar{x}$ (NOT), flips the value
\end{enumerate}

A \emph{literal} is either a variable or its negation, and a \emph{clause} is a disjunction of literals. For example:

\[
\underbrace{(x_1 + x_2)}_{\text{clause}} \cdot \underbrace{(\bar{x_1} + x_3)}_{\text{clause}}
\]

where $\underbrace{x_1}_{\text{literal}},\ \underbrace{x_2}_{\text{literal}},\ \underbrace{\bar{x_1}}_{\text{literal}},\ \underbrace{x_3}_{\text{literal}}$ are the literals.

Importanly, a formula is \emph{satisfiable} if there exists an assignment of values $\{0, 1\}$ to the variables that makes the formula evaluate to $1$ (true). The SAT problem asks:

\[
\text{SAT} = \{ \langle \phi \rangle \mid \phi \text{ is a satisfiable Boolean formula} \}
\]

\textbf{Example:}

Consider the formula:

\[
\phi = (x_1 + x_2) \cdot (\bar{x_1} + x_3) \cdot (\bar{x_2} + \bar{x_3})
\]

We try the assignment $x_1 = 1, x_2 = 0, x_3 = 1$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(x_1 + x_2)$ & $= (1 + 0) = 1$ \\
$(\bar{x_1} + x_3)$ & $= (0 + 1) = 1$ \\
$(\bar{x_2} + \bar{x_3})$ & $= (1 + 0) = 1$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Since all three clauses evaluate to $1$, the entire formula $\phi = 1$. Therefore this assignment \emph{satisfies} $\phi$, and thus $\langle \phi \rangle \in \text{SAT}$.


\subsection{3-SAT}

\emph{3-SAT} is a special case of SAT where every clause contains \emph{exactly} 3 literals. A \emph{literal} is either a variable $x_i$ or its negation $\bar{x_i}$. Formally:

\[
\text{3-SAT} = \{ \langle \phi \rangle \mid \phi \text{ is a satisfiable Boolean formula where every clause has exactly 3 literals} \}
\]

The difference between SAT and 3-SAT is:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
\textbf{SAT} & clauses can have \emph{any} number of literals \\
\textbf{3-SAT} & every clause has \emph{exactly} 3 literals \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\textbf{Example:}

Consider the formula:

\[
\phi = (x_1 + x_2 + x_3) \cdot (\bar{x_1} + \bar{x_2} + x_3) \cdot (x_1 + \bar{x_2} + \bar{x_3})
\]

where each clause has exactly 3 literals. We try the assignment $x_1 = 1, x_2 = 0, x_3 = 1$:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$(x_1 + x_2 + x_3)$ & $= (1 + 0 + 1) = 1$ \\
$(\bar{x_1} + \bar{x_2} + x_3)$ & $= (0 + 1 + 1) = 1$ \\
$(x_1 + \bar{x_2} + \bar{x_3})$ & $= (1 + 1 + 0) = 1$ \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Since all three clauses evaluate to $1$, the entire formula $\phi = 1$. Therefore this assignment \emph{satisfies} $\phi$, and thus $\langle \phi \rangle \in \text{3-SAT}$.

Now consider the formula:

\[
\psi = (x_1 + x_2 + x_3) \cdot (\bar{x_1} + \bar{x_2} + \bar{x_3}) \cdot (x_1 + \bar{x_2} + x_3) \cdot (\bar{x_1} + x_2 + \bar{x_3})
\]

One can verify that no assignment of $x_1, x_2, x_3 \in \{0, 1\}$ satisfies all four clauses simultaneously, so $\langle \psi \rangle \notin \text{3-SAT}$.

\subsection{Conjunctive Normal Form (CNF)}

Given sentence $L$ with atomic sentences $x_1, \dots, x_n$, we
construct an equivalent sentence $H$ in CNF.

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
Line & $x_1$ & $x_2$ & $\dots$ & $x_n$ & $L$ \\
\hline
1 & 1 & 1 & $\dots$ & 1 & $L_1$ \\
\hline
2 & 1 & 1 & $\dots$ & 0 & $L_2$ \\
\hline
3 & 1 & 0 & $\dots$ & 1 & $L_3$ \\
\hline
$\vdots$ & $\vdots$ & $\vdots$ & $\vdots$ & $\vdots$ & $\vdots$ \\
\hline
$2^n$ & 0 & 0 & $\dots$ & 0 & $L_{2^n}$ \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

\begin{enumerate}

\item Case 1:

When $L$ is true everywhere, it is a tautology. Any tautological
formula in CNF serves as an equivalent, such as $(p + \bar{p})$ or any
logically equivalent tautology.

\item Case 2:

When $L$ is false on some lines, for each line $j$ where $L = 0$,
construct clause $\phi_j$:

\[
\phi_j = \bigvee_{t=1}^{n} (\pm x_t)
\]

where

\[
(\pm x_t) =
\begin{cases}
\bar x_t, & \text{if } x_t = 1 \text{ on line } j, \\
x_t, & \text{if } x_t = 0 \text{ on line } j.
\end{cases}
\]

Each literal is the opposite of its truth value on line $j$, making
$\phi_j$ false only on line $j$.

Let $j_1, j_2, \dots, j_s$ index all lines where $L = 0$. Define:

\[
H = \phi_{j_1} \cdot \phi_{j_2} \cdot \dots \cdot \phi_{j_s} = \bigwedge_{i=1}^{s} \left[ \bigvee_{t=1}^{n} (\pm x_t) \right]
\]

$H$ is in CNF. Since $H$ is false exactly where $L$ is false, $L \equiv H$.

\end{enumerate}

\textbf{Example 1:}

Let

\[\Sigma = x_1 \to \bar{x}_2\]

First, we rewrite using the definition of implication:

\[\Sigma = (x_1 \to \bar{x}_2 \equiv \bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2)\]

Truth Table:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
$x_1$ & $x_2$ & $\bar{x}_1$ & $\bar{x}_2$ & $\Sigma$ \\
\hline
1 & 1 & 0 & 0 & 0 \\
\hline
1 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 1 & 1 & 0 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 0 & 1 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

CNF Construction:

\begin{enumerate}
\item Line 1: $(\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2)$
\end{enumerate}

Thus,

\[\Sigma = (\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2)\]

\textbf{Example 2:}

Let

\[\psi = \overline{(x_1 \equiv x_2)}\]

First, we rewrite using the definition of biconditional:

\[x_1 \leftrightarrow x_2 \equiv (x_1 \cdot x_2) + (\bar{x}_1 \cdot \bar{x}_2)\]

Then, applying negation:

\[
\overline{(x_1 \leftrightarrow x_2)} \equiv \overline{[(x_1 \cdot x_2) + (\bar{x}_1 \cdot \bar{x}_2)]}
\]

Using De Morgan's law:

\[
\psi = (\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2) \cdot (x_1 + x_2)
\]

Truth Table:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
$x_1$ & $x_2$ & $\bar{x}_1$ & $\bar{x}_2$ & $x_1 \equiv x_2$ & $\psi$ \\
\hline
1 & 1 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 0 \\
\hline
1 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 0 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 1 & 1 & 0 & 0 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 0 & 1 & 1 & 1 & 0 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

CNF Construction:

$\psi = 0$ on lines 1 and 4.

\begin{enumerate}
\item Line 1: $(\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2)$
\item Line 4: $(x_1 + x_2)$
\end{enumerate}

Thus,

\[
\psi = (\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2) \cdot (x_1 + x_2)
\]

\textbf{Example 3:}

Let

\[\delta = \bar{x}_1 \lor \overline{(x_2 \land x_3)}\]

First, we apply De Morgan's law to $\overline{(x_2 \land x_3)}$:

\[\overline{(x_2 \land x_3)} \equiv \bar{x}_2 + \bar{x}_3\]

Then, the expression becomes:

\[
\delta = \bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2 + \bar{x}_3
\]

Truth Table:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
$x_1$ & $x_2$ & $x_3$ & $\bar{x}_1$ & $x_2 \land x_3$ & $\overline{(x_2 \land x_3)}$ & $\delta$ \\
\hline
1 & 1 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 0 & 0 \\
\hline
1 & 1 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
1 & 0 & 1 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
1 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 1 & 1 & 1 & 1 & 0 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 0 & 1 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

CNF Construction:

$\delta = 0$ on line 1 only.

\begin{enumerate}
\item Line 1: $(\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2 + \bar{x}_3)$
\end{enumerate}

Thus,

\[
\delta = \bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2 + \bar{x}_3
\]


\textbf{Example 4:}

Let

\[\zeta = \overline{(x_1 \lor x_2)} \leftrightarrow ((\bar{x}_3 \land \bar{x}_1) \to \bar{x}_2)\]

First, we rewrite using the definition of implication and biconditional:

\[\overline{(x_1 \lor x_2)} \equiv \bar{x}_1 \cdot \bar{x}_2\]

\[(\bar{x}_3 \land \bar{x}_1) \to \bar{x}_2 \equiv \overline{(\bar{x}_3 \cdot \bar{x}_1)} + \bar{x}_2 \equiv (x_3 + x_1) + \bar{x}_2 \equiv x_3 + x_1 + \bar{x}_2\]

Truth Table:

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|c|c|c|}
\hline
$x_1$ & $x_2$ & $x_3$ & $\overline{(x_1 \lor x_2)}$ & $(\bar{x}_3 \land \bar{x}_1) \to \bar{x}_2$ & $\zeta$ \\
\hline
1 & 1 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 0 \\
\hline
1 & 1 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 0 \\
\hline
1 & 0 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 0 \\
\hline
1 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 0 \\
\hline
0 & 1 & 1 & 0 & 1 & 0 \\
\hline
0 & 1 & 0 & 0 & 0 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 0 & 1 & 1 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
0 & 0 & 0 & 1 & 1 & 1 \\
\hline
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

CNF Construction:

$\zeta = 0$ on lines 1, 2, 3, 4, and 5.

\begin{enumerate}
\item Line 1: $(\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2 + \bar{x}_3)$
\item Line 2: $(\bar{x}_1 + \bar{x}_2 + x_3)$
\item Line 3: $(\bar{x}_1 + x_2 + \bar{x}_3)$
\item Line 4: $(\bar{x}_1 + x_2 + x_3)$
\item Line 5: $(x_1 + \bar{x}_2 + \bar{x}_3)$
\end{enumerate}

Simplified form:

\[
\zeta = (\bar{x}_1) \cdot (\bar{x}_2 + \bar{x}_3)
\]

\section{The P versus NP}

\begin{center}
\begin{tabular}{ll}
$\mathbf{P}$ & the class of languages for which membership can be \emph{decided} quickly. \\
$\mathbf{NP}$ & the class of languages for which membership can be \emph{verified} quickly. \\
\end{tabular}
\end{center}

Languages such as $\text{HAMPATH}$ and $\text{CLIQUE}$ are known to be in $\mathbf{NP}$, yet no polynomial time algorithm has been found for either. 
This suggests that polynomial verifiability is strictly more powerful than polynomial decidability. However, we cannot rule out the possibility that $\mathbf{P} = \mathbf{NP}$, as no one has proven the existence of a single language in $\mathbf{NP}$ that is provably not in $\mathbf{P}$.
Whether $\mathbf{P} = \mathbf{NP}$ remains one of the deepest open problems in mathematics and computer science. A positive answer would mean that every problem whose solution can be checked quickly can also be solved quickly.

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}
  \draw (0,0) ellipse (2cm and 1cm);
  \draw (0,0) ellipse (1cm and 0.5cm);
  \node at (0,0) {$\mathbf{P}$};
  \node at (0,0.75) {$\mathbf{NP}$};
  \node at (0,-1.4) {If $\mathbf{P} \neq \mathbf{NP}$};

  \draw (5,0) ellipse (1cm and 0.5cm);
  \node at (5,0) {$\mathbf{P} = \mathbf{NP}$};
  \node at (5,-1.4) {If $\mathbf{P} = \mathbf{NP}$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\section{Polynomial Time Reducibility}

A language $X$ is \emph{polynomial-time reducible} to a language $Y$, written $X \leq_P Y$, if there exists a polynomial-time computable function $f$ such that:

\[
x \in X \leftrightarrow f(x) \in Y
\]

The function $f$ is called a \emph{polynomial-time reduction} from $X$ to $Y$. This means:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $f$ is computable in polynomial time.
    \item If $x \in X$, then $f(x) \in Y$.
    \item If $x \notin X$, then $f(x) \notin Y$.
\end{enumerate}

Intuitively, $X \leq_P Y$ means that $Y$ is \emph{at least as hard} as $X$, if we can solve $Y$ in polynomial time, then we can also solve $X$ in polynomial time by first applying $f$ to transform the input, then solving $Y$.

\section{The Cook-Levin Theorem}

The Cook-Levin Theorem states that SAT is NP-complete. That is:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item $\text{SAT} \in \mathbf{NP}$, and
    \item every $X \in \mathbf{NP}$ satisfies $X \leq_P \text{SAT}$.
\end{enumerate}

This was established independently by Stephen Cook in 1971 and Leonid Levin in 1973. The significance of this theorem is:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item If SAT $\in \mathbf{P}$, then $\mathbf{P} = \mathbf{NP}$.
    \item SAT serves as the \emph{root} of all NP-completeness proofs, meaning to show any problem $Y$ is NP-complete, it suffices to show $\text{SAT} \leq_P Y$.
\end{enumerate}

This gives rise to the reduction chain:

\[
\text{SAT} \leq_P \text{3-SAT} \leq_P \text{CLIQUE} \leq_P \text{VERTEX-COVER} \leq_P \text{INDEPENDENT-SET}
\]

Let $N$ be a NTM that decides some language $X \in \mathbf{NP}$ in time $n^k$. We construct a Boolean formula $\chi$ such that $\chi$ is satisfiable if and only if $N$ accepts input $\alpha$.
A \emph{tableau} for $N$ on $\alpha$ is an $n^k \times n^k$ grid where:

\begin{enumerate}
    \item Each \emph{row} represents a configuration of $N$.
    \item Each \emph{column} represents a tape cell at a given time step.
    \item Row 1 is the start configuration.
    \item The last row is the $n^k$-th configuration.
\end{enumerate}

\begin{center}
\begin{tikzpicture}
  \draw (0,0) rectangle (6,6);
  \draw (0,5) -- (6,5);
  \draw (0,4.5) -- (6,4.5);
  \draw (0,0.5) -- (6,0.5);
  \draw (1,6) -- (1,0);
  \draw (1.5,6) -- (1.5,0);
  \draw (2,6) -- (2,0);
  \draw (5,6) -- (5,0);
  \draw (5.5,6) -- (5.5,0);

  \node at (0.5,5.5) {\#};
  \node at (1.25,5.5) {$w_0$};
  \node at (1.75,5.5) {$\alpha_1$};
  \node at (2.5,5.5) {$\alpha_2$};
  \node at (3.5,5.5) {$\cdots$};
  \node at (4.5,5.5) {$\alpha_n$};
  \node at (5.25,5.5) {$\scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace}$};
  \node at (5.75,5.5) {\#};
  \node[right] at (6,5.5) {start configuration};

  \node at (0.5,4.75) {\#};
  \node at (5.75,4.75) {\#};
  \node[right] at (6,4.75) {second configuration};

  \node at (0.5,0.25) {\#};
  \node at (3.5,0.25) {$ \ldots \ w_{\text{accept}} \ \ldots$};
  \node at (5.75,0.25) {\#};
  \node[right] at (6,0.25) {$n^k$-th configuration};

  \draw (2.5,2) rectangle (4.5,3.5);
  \draw (3,2) -- (3,3.5);
  \draw (3.5,2) -- (3.5,3.5);
  \draw (4,2) -- (4,3.5);
  \draw (2.5,2.75) -- (4.5,2.75);
  \node[right] at (6,2.75) {window};
  \draw[->] (6,2.75) -- (4.5,2.75);

  \draw[<-] (-0.3,0) -- (-0.3,2.8);
  \draw[->] (-0.3,3.2) -- (-0.3,6);
  \node[left] at (-0.3,3) {$n^k$};
  \draw[<-] (0,-0.3) -- (2.8,-0.3);
  \draw[->] (3.2,-0.3) -- (6,-0.3);
  \node[below] at (3,-0.3) {$n^k$};
\end{tikzpicture}
\end{center}

\begin{enumerate}
    \item For each cell $(i,j)$ in the tableau and each symbol $s \in C$ where $C = W \cup \Gamma \cup \{\#\}$, we define a Boolean variable:
    \[
    x_{i,j,s} = 1 \leftrightarrow \text{cell } (i,j) \text{ contains symbol } s
    \]

    \item We construct $\chi$ as:
    \[
    \chi = \chi_{\text{cell}} \cdot \chi_{\text{start}} \cdot \chi_{\text{accept}} \cdot \chi_{\text{move}}
    \]

    where each component is defined as follows:

    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\chi_{\text{cell}}$: Every cell contains exactly one symbol:
        
        \[
        \chi_{\text{cell}} = \bigwedge_{1 \leq i,j \leq n^k} \left[ \left( \bigvee_{s \in C} x_{i,j,s} \right) \cdot \left( \bigwedge_{\substack{s,t \in C \\ s \neq t}} \overline{x_{i,j,s} \cdot x_{i,j,t}} \right) \right]
        \]

        \item $\chi_{\text{start}}$: The first row is the start configuration $\#w_0\, \alpha_1\, \alpha_2 \cdots \alpha_n \scalebox{2.5}{\textvisiblespace} \cdots \#$:
        
        \[
        \chi_{\text{start}} = x_{1,1,\#} \cdot x_{1,2,w_0} \cdot x_{1,3,\alpha_1} \cdots x_{1,n+2,\alpha_n} \cdot x_{1,n^k,\#}
        \]

        \item $\chi_{\text{accept}}$: Some row contains the accept state $w_{\text{accept}}$:
        
        \[
        \chi_{\text{accept}} = \bigvee_{1 \leq i,j \leq n^k} x_{i,j,w_{\text{accept}}}
        \]

        \item $\chi_{\text{move}}$: Every $2 \times 3$ window in the tableau is legal, i.e.\ it corresponds to a valid transition of $N$:
        
        \[
        \chi_{\text{move}} = \bigwedge_{1 \leq i,j \leq n^k} \left( \text{the window at } (i,j) \text{ is legal} \right)
        \]

        A $2 \times 3$ window is \emph{legal} if it corresponds to a valid transition of $N$. $V(w_0, \triangle) = \{(w_0, \square, R)\}$ and $V(w_0, \square) = \{(w_1, \Diamond, L), (w_1, \triangle, R)\}$.

        Legal windows:

        \begin{center}
        \begin{tabular}{ccc}

        (a) &
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
        \hline
        $\triangle$ & $w_0$ & $\square$ \\
        \hline
        $w_1$ & $\triangle$ & $\Diamond$ \\
        \hline
        \end{tabular}
        &
        (b)
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
        \hline
        $\triangle$ & $w_0$ & $\square$ \\
        \hline
        $\triangle$ & $\triangle$ & $w_1$ \\
        \hline
        \end{tabular}
        \\[1.5em]

        (c) &
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
        \hline
        \# & $\square$ & $\triangle$ \\
        \hline
        \# & $\square$ & $\triangle$ \\
        \hline
        \end{tabular}
        &
        (d)
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
        \hline
        $\triangle$ & $\square$ & $\triangle$ \\
        \hline
        $\triangle$ & $\square$ & $w_1$ \\
        \hline
        \end{tabular}

        \end{tabular}
        \end{center}

        Illegal windows:

        \begin{center}
        \begin{tabular}{ccc}

        (a) &
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
        \hline
        $\triangle$ & $\triangle$ & $w_0$ \\
        \hline
        $\triangle$ & $\triangle$ & $\square$ \\
        \hline
        \end{tabular}
        &
        (b)
        \begin{tabular}{|c|c|c|}
        \hline
        $\square$ & $\square$ & $\square$ \\
        \hline
        $\Diamond$ & $\square$ & $\square$ \\
        \hline
        \end{tabular}

        \end{tabular}
        \end{center}
    \end{enumerate}

    \item The tableau has an accepting computation if and only if $\chi$ is satisfiable:
    \begin{enumerate}
        \item $\chi_{\text{cell}} = 1$ ensures every cell has exactly one symbol.
        \item $\chi_{\text{start}} = 1$ ensures the first row is the start configuration.
        \item $\chi_{\text{accept}} = 1$ ensures some row reaches $q_{\text{accept}}$.
        \item $\chi_{\text{move}} = 1$ ensures each row legally follows from the previous.
    \end{enumerate}

    \item The tableau has $n^{2k}$ cells, each with $|C|$ variables, so the total number of variables is $O(n^{2k})$. Each of the four components is polynomial in $n^k$, so $|\chi|$ is polynomial in $n$. 
    Therefore the reduction runs in polynomial time.
\end{enumerate}
